ATP 100 COMMERCIAL TRANSACTIONSFIRM 33QUESTION 7: Outline the legal and structural considerations about the merger of two limited liability companies in Kenya. (20 marks)This Photo by Unknown Author is licensed under CC BY-SA-NCThis Photo by Unknown Author is licensed under CC BY-SA IntroductionMergers in Kenya are regulated by various statutes and bodies. As such, there are legal requirements which the merging parties will have to consider in accordance with those statutes and regulator‚Äôs regulations. The structure of the merger deal is itself a great consideration since it may form part of the internal or economic determination of whether to merge in the first place. This group will therefore start by looking at the statutory provisions governing mergers and identify the legal and structural considerations arising from each. We will the conclude by discussing any other considerations particularly those touching on the structure of the deal itself. LEGAL CONSIDERATIONSThe Companies Act (No. 17 of 2015)- Part XXXV, Division 2 -Sections 933 to 947 Section 933 ‚Äì Applies to public companies which can merge in either two ways:by absorption (where one is subsumed in the other and ceases to exist in its own name); and creation of a new company-can either be private or public. Section 934 - the draft terms of the proposed merger to be prepared.Section 935 - a notice of the lodgement of the terms to the registrar be published in the Gazette at least a month before the shareholder‚Äôs a
... [Content truncated]

--- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
Incorporation Companies (and limited liability partnerships) are formed via a formal process known as ‚Äòincorporation‚Äô. The vast majority of companies are incorporated by registration, which involves registering certain documents with the Registrar of Companies at Sheria House. A company can do many things that a natural person can, including entering into contracts, owning property and commencing legal proceedings. In the vast majority of companies, the liability of the members will be limited. A company can be held liable for a civil wrong, or found guilty of committing a crime. A company has corporate personality, which means that, at law, it is a person. The courts can set aside a company‚Äôs corporate personality where statute so provides, or where a company is interposed in order to evade, or frustrate the enforcement of, a legal obligation. Introduction Sole proprietorships and ordinary partnerships can be brought into existence very easily and with minimal state involvement. Conversely, companies (and LLPs) are brought into existence at the discretion of the state via a formal process known as ‚Äòincorporation‚Äô. The word ‚Äòincorporation‚Äô is used because successful incorporation brings into existence a ‚Äòcorporation‚Äô (s 18-19 of the CA 2015 ‚Äì effects of incorporation. Methods of incorporation There are three principal methods by which a company can be incorporated: incorporation by Act of Parliament incorporation by Executive Order incorporation by registration It should be n
... [Content truncated]

New Approaches The decision of the Court of Appeal in Adams v Cape Industries plc clearly indicates that the courts are not quick to pierce the veil and the number of grounds upon which the veil can be pierced is limited. The Supreme Court case of Petrodel Resources Ltd v Prest [2013] has further limited the grounds on which the veil can be pierced by stating that there is only one instance in which the veil can be pierced and, even in that instance, the veil will only be pierced if it is necessary to do so. Prest must now be regarded as the leading case in this area and casts significant doubt on the validity of many of the cases discussed (at least in terms of them being regarded as instances of piercing the veil). Petrodel Resources Ltd v Prest [2013] UKSC 34 FACTS: The case involved a divorce settlement between Mr and Mrs Prest. The High Court had awarded Mrs Prest a divorce settlement totalling ¬£17.5 million, but much of Mr Prest‚Äôs assets were (p. 39) tied up in companies that were solely controlled by him. Section 24(1)(a) of the Matrimonial Causes Act 1973 grants the court the power to ‚Äòorder that a party to the marriage shall transfer to the other party ‚Ä¶ property to which the first mentioned party is entitled‚Äô. The High Court utilized this power to pierce the corporate veils of these companies and order the relevant properties to be transferred to Mrs Prest. Mr Prest appealed, questioning whether the court had the power to do this given that the properties did not be

Directors Key facts Every private company must have at least one director and every public company must have at least two directors. The power to manage the company is initially vested in the members, but is usually delegated to the directors by the articles. Directors‚Äô duties are now found in the Companies Act 2015, which provides for seven general duties that directors owe to the company. The general duties are based on prior common law duties, so pre-2015 case law will remain relevant. Certain types of transaction involving directors will only be valid if authorized by a resolution of the members. A director‚Äôs term of office can terminate in several ways including resignation, retirement, or removal. Additionally, the courts can disqualify a person from acting as director. The definition of ‚Äòdirector‚Äô A company may be run by persons who call themselves ‚Äògovernors‚Äô or ‚Äòmanagers‚Äô and, increasingly, persons not involved in management at board level are called ‚Äòdirectors‚Äô. Are such persons actually directors? The CA 2015 s3 defines a director as follows: in relation to a body corporate, includes‚Äî any person occupying the position of a director of the body (by whatever name the person is called); and any person in accordance with whose directions or instructions (not being advice given in a professional capacity) the directors of the body are accustomed to act; This broad definition will cover those who have been validly appointed to the office of director (known as de jure (‚Äòi

Members Although the terms ‚Äòmember‚Äô and ‚Äòshareholder‚Äô tend to be used interchangeably, there is a distinction (especially in companies that do not have a share capital). Members usually exercise their decision-making powers via the passing of resolutions. A resolution is simply a formal vote. The two forms of resolution are ordinary resolutions (which require a simple majority) and special resolutions (which require a majority of no less than 75 per cent). Public companies must pass resolutions at general meetings. Private companies must pass resolutions at general meetings, unless the written resolution procedure is used. Resolutions are only valid if sufficient notice of the meeting is provided and a quorum is present. A quorum is simply the minimum number of persons required to be present in order to conduct business. Members can appoint a proxy to attend, speak, and vote at general meetings on their behalf. Introduction The members of a company play two vital roles. First, through the purchase of shares, they contribute capital to the company. The nature of the share and share capital is discussed in the next class but our focus is on the second vital role that the members play, namely their ability to make decisions. As will be seen, a significant amount of power is placed into the hands of members and numerous key decisions are reserved for them alone. However, before the members‚Äô ability to exercise this power can be discussed, it is important to note the difference be
... [Content truncated]

ATP 108 Commercial Transactions - 2024LECTURE 1 ‚Äì INTRODUCTION TO CORPORATE LAW ‚Äì FOUNDATIONAL CONCEPTSCourse Instructor ‚Äì Stephen Mallowah LLB, LLM, MSc Is there a common structure for legal business corporations? Are there similarities of legal characteristics of companies/ corporations in all jurisdictions? There are five characteristics that it is possible to observe in every company doing business:Legal personality;Limited liability;Transferable shares;Delegated management;Investor ownership.2 The five core structural characteristics of a CompanyIn most economically active jurisdictions there is a basic statute that provides for the formation of firms with all of the above-mentioned characteristics.These characteristics respond to the economic exigencies of the large modern business enterprise.Even if there are other forms of business enterprise that lack one or more of these characteristics, in market economies, almost all companies adopt a legal form that possess all of them. These characteristics make the company attractive for organizing productive activity, but also generate tensions and trade-offs that lead to the the agency problems that corporate law must deal with.3 1. The constitution of the company A company‚Äôs constitution consists primarily of the articles of association, and agreements and resolutions affecting the company‚Äôs constitution. The Companies Act 2015 has significantly reduced the importance of the memorandum of association, and the articles now form the company‚Äôs principal constitutional document. The constitution forms a statutory contract between the company and its members, and between the members themselves, but only those provisions relating to membership rights will constitute terms of the statutory contract. A company that acts outside the scope of its objects clause will be acting ultra vires. Companies incorporated under the Companies Act 2015 have unrestricted objects by default ‚Äì CA 2015 s28. A company can alter its articles by passing a special resolution, although statute and the common law restrict a company‚Äôs ability to alter its articles. Despite being the largest piece of legislation ever passed, the CA 2015 does not seek to exhaustively regulate the internal affairs of companies. Much is left to the companies themselves who will usually create their own internal rules via the company‚Äôs constitution. A company‚Äôs constitution largely fulfils the same function as the constitution of a country, namely, to set out the powers, rights and obligations of those who are subject to the constitution. Accordingly, a company‚Äôs constitution aims to set out the powers, rights and obligations of the company‚Äôs members and dir
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

2. Incorporation ¬ß Companies (and limited liability partnerships) are formed via a formal process known as ‚Äòincorporation‚Äô. ¬ß The vast majority of companies are incorporated by registration, which involves registering certain documents with the Registrar of Companies at Sheria House. ¬ß A company can do many things that a natural person can, including entering into contracts, owning property and commencing legal proceedings. ¬ß In the vast majority of companies, the liability of the members will be limited. ¬ß A company can be held liable for a civil wrong, or found guilty of committing a crime. ¬ß A company has corporate personality, which means that, at law, it is a person. ¬ß The courts can set aside a company‚Äôs corporate personality where statute so provides, or where a company is interposed in order to evade, or frustrate the enforcement of, a legal obligation. Introduction Sole proprietorships and ordinary partnerships can be brought into existence very easily and with minimal state involvement. Conversely, companies (and LLPs) are brought into existence at the discretion of the state via a formal process known as ‚Äòincorporation‚Äô. The word ‚Äòincorporation‚Äô is used because successful incorporation brings into existence a ‚Äòcorporation‚Äô (s 18-19 of the CA 2015 ‚Äì effects of incorporation. New Approaches The decision of the Court of Appeal in Adams v Cape Industries plc clearly indicates that the courts are not quick to pierce the veil and the number of grounds upon which the veil can be pierced is limited. The Supreme Court case of Petrodel Resources Ltd v Prest [2013] has further limited the grounds on which the veil can be pierced by stating that there is only one instance in which the veil can be pierced and, even in that instance, the veil will only be pierced if it is necessary to do so. Prest must now be regarded as the leading case in this area and casts significant doubt on the validity of many of the cases discussed (at least in terms of them being regarded as instances of piercing the veil). Petrodel Resources Ltd v Prest [2013] UKSC 34 FACTS: The case involved a divorce settlement between Mr and Mrs Prest. The High Court had awarded Mrs Prest a divorce settlement totalling ¬£17.5 million, but much of Mr Prest‚Äôs assets were (p. 39) tied up in companies that were solely controlled by him. Section 24(1)(a) of the Matrimonial Causes Act 1973 grants the court the power to ‚Äòorder that a party to the marriage shall transfer to the other party ‚Ä¶ property to which the first mentioned party is entitled‚Äô. The High Court utilized this power to pierce the corporate veils of these companies and order the relevant properties to be transferred to Mrs Prest. Mr Prest appealed, questioning whether the court had the power to do this given that the properties did not be

Capital and capital maintenance The two principal types of capital that companies acquire are share capital (capital obtained by selling shares) and debt capital (capital borrowed from others). A new allotment of shares must usually first be offered to existing shareholders. Companies are prohibited from allotting shares for less than their nominal value. Companies can issue different classes of shares with different class rights, and the law regulates the ability to vary such class rights. Public companies are subject to minimum capital requirements when commencing business, whereas private companies are not. A reduction of share capital is only valid if the strict procedures relating to a reduction of capital found in the Companies Act 2015 are complied with. Companies are generally prohibited from purchasing their own shares, but the 2015 Act does provide exceptions to this prohibition. Private companies are permitted to provide financial assistance to acquire their own shares. Public companies are generally prohibited from providing such assistance, but there are exceptions. Companies can only pay a dividend out of ‚Äòprofits available for thepurpose‚Äô. The two principal types of charge are fixed charges and floating charges and, in order to be enforceable, charges need to be registered in accordance with the Companies Act 2015. Shares and share capital The CA 2015 defines ‚Äòshares‚Äô as in relation to an undertaking with a share capital, means shares in the share capital of th
... [Content truncated]

Directors Key facts ¬ß Every private company must have at least one director and every public company must have at least two directors. ¬ß The power to manage the company is initially vested in the members, but is usually delegated to the directors by the articles. ¬ß Directors‚Äô duties are now found in the Companies Act 2015, which provides for seven general duties that directors owe to the company. ¬ß The general duties are based on prior common law duties, so pre-2015 case law will remain relevant. ¬ß Certain types of transaction involving directors will only be valid if authorized by a resolution of the members. ¬ß A director‚Äôs term of office can terminate in several ways including resignation, retirement, or removal. Additionally, the courts can disqualify a person from acting as director. The definition of ‚Äòdirector‚Äô A company may be run by persons who call themselves ‚Äògovernors‚Äô or ‚Äòmanagers‚Äô and, increasingly, persons not involved in management at board level are called ‚Äòdirectors‚Äô. Are such persons actually directors? Members ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß Although the terms ‚Äòmember‚Äô and ‚Äòshareholder‚Äô tend to be used interchangeably, there is a distinction (especially in companies that do not have a share capital). Members usually exercise their decision-making powers via the passing of resolutions. A resolution is simply a formal vote. The two forms of resolution are ordinary resolutions (which require a simple majority) and special resolutions (which require a majority of no less than 75 per cent). Public companies must pass resolutions at general meetings. Private companies must pass resolutions at general meetings, unless the written resolution procedure is used. Resolutions are only valid if sufficient notice of the meeting is provided and a quorum is present. A quorum is simply the minimum number of persons required to be present in order to conduct business. Members can appoint a proxy to attend, speak, and vote at general meetings on their behalf. Introduction The members of a company play two vital roles. First, through the purchase of shares, they contribute capital to the company. The nature of the share and share capital is discussed in the next class but our focus is on the second vital role that the members play, namely their ability to make decisions. As will be seen, a significant amount of power is placed into the hands of members and numerous key decisions are reserved for them alone. However, before the members‚Äô ability to exercise this power can be discussed, it is important to note the d

In fact, agreement can be defined as a meeting of minds with the understanding and acceptance of reciprocal legal rights and duties as to particular actions or obligations, which the parties intend to exchange or a mutual assent to do or refrain from doing something. It is important to understand that if we enter into agreements with commercial organizations we will have important obligations to fulfil in return for any rewards that we might receive. So before any commercial agreement is finally signed all parties should invest considerable time and effort in the process which can take many months. This book‚Äôs extensive coverage of most of the types of business agreements that we are likely to encounter in the commercial sphere enables the readers to understand the mode of drafting agreements and contracts in a simple and understandable manner. Company Insolvency Options Receivership A receiver is appointed by a secured creditor to deal with the secured assets. A company can be in receivership and also in liquidation or voluntary administration. Voluntary administration An administrator is appointed to review and rearrange a business to avoid liquidation. Liquidation A liquidator is appointed to investigate and deal with all the business assets. The creditors can apply to the High Court for this to happen (Creditor Liquidation), or the shareholders can pass a special resolution (Voluntary Liquidation). The liquidator is usually a chartered accountant, or an insolvency expert. Note The Insolvency and Trustee Service only deals with liquidations, and generally only if appointed by the court. The Liquidation Process This process can be different depending on the circumstances, e.g. whether the company is trading, or depending on what assets it has. Company is unable to pay its creditors The company is unable to pay its creditors and the company or the shareholders have it placed into liquidation. A liquidator is appointed The liquidator notifies The BRS, and advertises the appointment. Creditors‚Äô meeting A creditors‚Äô meeting may be held to appoint a replacement liquidator. The administration of the liquidation starts. This may include; closing the business identifying and selling the company assets contacting and receiving claims from the creditors sending progress reports to the creditors investigating possible offenc

TRAINING MODULE Table of Contents NAME SEARCH During the registering a new company, the first step is to get a name for the company. This involves making an application though e-citizen website, to reserve the name. Before conducting a name search the following should be observed: That there is an application for reservation of a company name. A receipt for payment/invoice of the prescribed fee. The Registrar may, on written application, reserve a name pending the registration of the company1, through online search at the Companies Registry. The name is reserved for a minimum of 30 days, as the registrar confirms whether the name is being used by another company. The period may be extended for another 30 days, for special reasons, to give the applicant time to register the name2. One is then required to pay a fee of Ksh 150 per name, which is broken down to Ksh1003 for the name and Ksh50 as the convenience fee. It should be noted that there is no requirement for a minimum or maximum number of names that an application can give. Choosing of a suitable company Name One must choose a company name for the business that he/she intends to run. The name cannot be the same as another company in the index of registered companies (Section 57 of Companies Act, 2015). The Business Registration Service department ensures that the names being lodged for registration do not constitute any prohibited names such as ‚ÄúAl Shabab, Mungiki‚Äù. THE BANK CUSTOMER RELATIONSHIP AND NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS A LECTURE PRESENTED AT THE KENYA SCHOOL OF LAW DANSON KIRITU, ADVOCATE LEGAL MANAGER, HEAD OFFICE & INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS KCB BANK KENYA LIMITED What is a 'Bank' A bank is a financial institution licensed to receive deposits and issue financial facilities including loans, guarantees and letters of credit. Banks may also provide financial services, such as wealth management, currency exchange and safe deposit boxes. There are two types of banks: commercial/retail banks and investment banks. In this country, banks are regulated by the central bank. Commercial banks are typically concerned with managing withdrawals and receiving deposits as well as supplying short-term loans to individuals and small businesses. Consumers primarily use these banks for basic checking and savings accounts, certificates of deposit (CDs) and home mortgages. Examples of commercial banks include KCB Bank, Barclays Bank of Kenya and Cooperative Bank Investment banks focus on providing corporate clients with services such as underwriting and assisting with merger and acquisition (M&A) activity. KCB Capital, Dyer and Blair, Kingdom Securities are examples of investment banks. Central banks are chiefly responsible for currency stability, controlling inflation and monetary policy and overseeing money supply. In re Abdallah Chitechi Munyendo [2022] eKLR REPUBLIC OF KENYA IN THE HIGH COURT OF KENYA AT KAKAMEGA BANKRUPTY CAUSE No. 1 OF 2018 IN THE MATTER OF THE MATTER OF THE INSOLVENCY ACT No 18 of 2015 AND IN THE MATTER OF ABDALLAH CHITECHI MUNYENDO RULING 1. The Petitioner before the Court petitioning for bankruptcy is the Debtor himself, Abdallah Chitechi Munyendo. Section 13 of the Insolvency Act No 18 of 2015 (hereinafter referred to as ‚Äúthe Act‚Äù) envisages such a petition. It is to be made in accordance with Section 32 of the Act. The only ground under which the Court can make an order for bankruptcy is that the debtor is unable to pay his/her debts. 2. Before the Court can exercise its jurisdiction, it must be satisfied that the Debtor has complied with Section 32(2) of the Act, in particular that the Application must be accompanied by a statement of the debtor‚Äôs financial position. The Statement of Financial Position must contain the following: (a) Such particulars of the debtor‚Äôs creditors and of the debtor‚Äôs debts and other liabilities and assets as may be prescribed by the insolvency regulations; and (b) Such other information as may be so prescribed. 3. The importance of the Statement of Financial Position can be seen by the fact that sub-section (3) provides that the Court may reject a statement of the debtor‚Äôs financial position if of the opinion that it is incorrect or incomplete. Bowie Bonds: A Look at David Bowie's Innovative Estate Plan At the time of his tragic and unexpected (to the public, anyway) death, the iconic David Bowie had it all: family, fame, and fortune. Not so long ago, however, the artist found himself increasingly under pressure as financial obligations began to overcome a somewhat unpredictable music career. Eager to overcome his fiscal problems while retaining ultimate rights to his music, Bowie devised an ingenious plan that accomplished both goals, all while creating a lasting intellectual property dynasty that will benefit his surviving wife and children for years to come. Known as "Bowie Bonds", his innovative plan has transformed the transactional side of the music industry and helped to protect vulnerable artists from irrevocably signing away the rights to their creations during periods of financial uncertainty. The concept he pioneered has been embraced by elite artists and securities investors worldwide. Intellectual Property Bonds In 1997, amid growing concerns of physical and financial security, David Bowie met with a group of financial analysts to devise an investment vehicle that would both protect his artistic achievements and ensure regular income and protection from a somewhat volatile and unpredictable industry: show business. After meeting with several inventive financial professionals, Bowie opted to create an asset-backed security resting on the value of his pre-1990 portfolio of artistic works, which consisted 
... [Content truncated]

DISQUALIFICATION OF DIRECTORS (CONT.)AG‚Äôs powers to disqualify ‚Äì The AG is empowered to apply to disqualify officers of a solvent company if such officer was acting in the capacity when the company was declared insolvent. He may also make such application on a matter of public interest.Persons disqualified in non-Kenyan jurisdictions are also deemed to be disqualified from acting in connection with Kenyan companies.Effect: A disqualified person will cease to be director or secretary of a company, will not be authorised to act as a liquidator or administrator with regard to the company or supervise any voluntary arrangement entered into by the company. A disqualification prohibits the person from being involved with the promotion, formation or management of the company, directly and indirectly.The maximum period of disqualification will be 5 years if convicted before a magistrates court, and 15 years of convicted before any other court.Breach of any disqualification orders or undertakings attracts a fine of KES 1,000,000 or a prison term not exceeding 5 years or both.24Changes in Company Secretarial Practice and Compliance MEMBERS (Cont.)Information rights and exercise of members rights by othersIndirect investors rights:Companies may provide in their articles for members to nominate another person(s) to enjoy or exercise their rights as a member (other than the right to transfer the shares)Õæ orRegistered members of companies traded on a registered exchange to nominate another

COMPANY LAW DEFINITION Section 2 of the Companies Act defines a company as an association of people for a common object or objects. Thus unlike a Partnership which is an association of persons who come together for business with a view of profit, the objects of a company extend beyond profit making. A company is different from a partnership in that in a partnership, each partner is liable for COMPANIES AND PARTNERSHIPS the debts and liabilities of the firm without limits, except for limited partnerships. Second, a partnership has no separate existence from the partners; third, a partnership has no perpetual succession unlike a company, so that the death, retirement or bankruptcy of a partner ends it. it. Section 389 of the Companies Act provides COMPANIES AND PARTNERHIPS that no association or partnership consisting of more than 20 persons may be formed for the purposes of carrying on any business for the acquisition of gains unless it is registered under the Companies Act, or is formed pursuant to some other statute. Section 2 of the Companies Act 2015 provides that a company is a company formed and registered under the Act or an existing company. It also provides that one natural person may incorporate a company. Companies may be statutory companies which are formed either by specific Acts of Parliament such as Kenya Ports Authority Act, Kenya Railways Corporation Act, etc, (statutory corporations), or State corporations (parastatals) which are formed TYPES OF COMPANIES und
... [Content truncated]

CORPORATE INSOLVENCY: LIQUIDATION OF REGISTERED COMPANIES Class E: Firm 7 This topic will focus on the key corporate insolvency procedures of liquidation, administration and voluntary arrangements of companies under the Insolvency Act, 2015; highlighting the role of the government, regulators, the insolvency practitioner and other stakeholders including the duties and conduct of directors in relation to business in financial difficulty. Liquidation is a proceeding by means of which a company is dissolved and in the course of such dissolution its assets are collected and its debts are paid off out of the assets of the company or from contributions by its members, if necessary. If any surplus is left, it is distributed among the members in accordance with their rights. Section 384 of the Insolvency Act provides that a company is unable to pay its debts if- if a creditor (by assignment or otherwise) to whom the company is indebted for hundred thousand shillings or more has served on the company, by leaving it at the company's registered office, a written demand requiring the company to pay the debt and the company has for twenty-one days afterwards failed to pay the debt or to secure or compound for it to the reasonable satisfaction of the creditor; if execution or other process issued on a judgment, decree or order of any court in favour of a creditor of the company is returned unsatisfied in whole or in part; or if it is proved to the satisfaction of the Court that the company

Circumstances in which a company may be liquidated voluntarily(a) When the period (if any) fixed for the duration of the company by the articles expires(b) The event (if any) occurs, on the occurrence of which the articles provide that the company is to be dissolved, and the company in general meeting has passed a resolution providing for its voluntary liquidation; or (c) If the company resolves by special resolution that it be liquidated voluntarily.However, before passing a resolution for voluntary liquidation, the company shall give notice of the resolution to the holder of any qualifying floating charge in respect of the company's property. Appointment Of Special Managers A special manager is an officer of the court. The power of the special manager to exercise functions is conferred by the court and the special manager exercises them as a principal and appointee of the court. The official receiver should not request a special manager to perform acts in respect of which the official receiver has no statutory right to undertake himself/herself, nor should the official receiver request a special manager to discharge any responsibility outside the powers granted to him/her by the court. The PGN provides a framework for the establishment of a Regulatory Sandbox to allow for the live testing of new products, services, and solutions that have the potential to deepen the Kenya‚Äôs capital markets. On 6th January 2011, the Claimant filed a Memorandum of Claim, through which he sued the Respondent for unfair termination of employment. The Respondent filed a Memorandum of Response and Counterclaim on 20th May 2011 and the Claimant filed a Supplementary Memorandum of Claim on 8th October 2012. The Claimant worked as such until 27th October 2010 when the Respondent terminated his employment without lawful cause and without notice. He was not issued with a termination letter. SPECIAL ISSUE Kenya Gazette Supplement No. 45 273 18th March, 2022 (Legislative Supplement No. 23) LEGAL NOTICE NO. 46 THE CENTRAL BANK OF KENYA ACT (Cap. 491) IN EXERCISE of the powers conferred by section 57(1), (3) and (4) of the Central Bank of Kenya Act, the Central Bank of Kenya makes the following Regulations‚Äî THE CENTRAL BANK OF KENYA (DIGITAL CREDIT PROVIDERS) REGULATIONS, 2022 PART I‚Äî PRELIMINARY 1. These Regulations may be cited as the Central Bank of Kenya Citation. (Digital Credit Providers) Regulations, 2022. 2. These Regulations shall not apply to‚Äî Application. CHAPTER 1 Conducting Due Diligence: An Overview Risk has always been a companion of reward, inherent in assessing opportunities against a company‚Äôs strengths and weaknesses. ‚ÄîReport of the NACD Blue Ribbon Commission on Risk Governance (2009) INTRODUCTION The due diligence phase of any acquisition‚Äîthe study of the risks that the deal poses‚Äîcan make the difference between success and failure. Many a deal that looks good on the back of an envelope looks bad in a spreadsheet. Acquirers must face all the facts‚Äîeven worst-case scenarios. This is why due diligence must be considered in conjunction with other acquisition activities and goals, including strategic planning, valuation, financing, structuring, and‚Äîmost important‚Äîcharting the future of the combined company, with all its new risks and opportunities. To put due diligence in its proper perspective, we begin this chapter with the legal foundations of due diligence. Next, we discuss the ‚Äúwhat, when, and who‚Äù of due diligence‚Äîthat is, its scope, duration, and key participants. We then give some further background on the legal aspects of due diligence (focusing on ‚Äúdue diligence‚Äù in the securities law context). Finally, we close with suggestions for verification and risk prevention. Our goal is to build a strong platform of understanding for the more detailed work ahead. CHAPTER 4 The Legal Compliance Review Are we disposed to be the number of those who, having eyes, see not, and having ears, hear not . . . ? For my part, whatever anguish of spirit it may cost, I am willing to know the whole truth; to know the worst, and to provide for it. ‚ÄîPatrick Henry, Speech at the Virginia Convention, Richmond, March 23, 1775 INTRODUCTION Legal compliance begins at home, with a responsible due diligence process that uncovers current and potential causes of financial, operational, and legal problems. This process itself can eliminate about a quarter of potential suits against directors and officers. According to past research by Tillinghast Insurance, a unit of Towers Watson, one out of every four lawsuits filed against officers and directors in any 10-year period stems from a merger that has gone bad.1 Plaintiffs allege that the buyer or seller committed errors in the purchase or sale of the company. A responsible due diligence process, well documented, can provide a strong defense against such legal actions. It is somewhat more difficult, however, to anticipate and manage the other causes of lawsuits against corporate officers and directors. These can come from anyone over any issue‚Äîand legal claims often come in clusters, involving multiple issues.2 This chapter is about that vast terrain of potential litigation, one that expands whenever an acquirer buys another company. Why? Because the seller‚Äôs legal vulnerabilities are usually transferred to the bu

KN LAW LLP LEGAL March 17, 2020 Changes in Competition Law in Kenya Introduction The Competition (Amendment) Act, 2019 (the ‚ÄúAct‚Äù) was assented to by the President on December 13, 2019 to commence on December 31, 2019. The Act introduced amendments to the Competition Act to regulate abuse of buyer power in the market. Additionally, the Competition (General) Rules, 2019 (‚ÄúRules‚Äù) made under Section 93 of the Act, were gazetted on December 06, 2019. The Rules consolidate the informal practice guidelines issued by Competition Authority of Kenya (CAK). We discuss the changes introduced by the Act and the Rules below. The Competition (Amendment) Act, 2019 The amendments reorganize the provisions of the Act on buyer power and abuse of buyer power. They increase CAK‚Äôs scope of powers in preventing abuse of buyer power by enabling it to monitor industries and sectors in which abuse of power is likely to occur. CAK may also require a sector or undertaking to develop a binding code of practice. The CAK is then required to publish the code of practice. The implication to businesses is that companies in ‚Äòmonitored‚Äô sectors will be required to comply with reporting and prudential requirements. What is buyer power? It is the influence exerted by an undertaking in the position of purchaser to either obtain from a supplier more favourable terms or impose a long-term opportunity cost including harm or withheld benefit which would be significantly disproportionate to any resulting long-term co
... [Content truncated]

Historical background‚Ä¶continuedb. Corporate insolvencyIt is a recent phenomenonRemedy to corporate insolvency can be traced back to Joint Stock Companies Act of 1844. i. Joint Stock Companies Act of 1844The 1844 Act incorporated companies that were registered its provisions, and regulated the winding up of joint stock companies. ii. Winding-up Act of 1844Was the first statute enacted to regulate the winding up of companies.Before this Act was passed, the creditors could proceed against a company‚Äôs properties as well as the properties of shareholders.With the enactment of this statute, the creditors‚Äô rights were limited to the company‚Äôs property.The purpose of the Act was to force companies into bankruptcy just like natural persons. Historical Background‚Ä¶continueda. BankruptcyThe individual insolvency, also known as bankruptcy dominated the early history of England and Wales. Corporate insolvency is a recent phenomenon because limited liability companies were only recently conceived as juristic persons (Salmon vs Salmon)Can be traced back to the law which was applied to the Italian medieval city states. Bankruptcy law then was applicable to the merchants and had its roots in Roman law.The early bankruptcy laws in England, just like the Italian city states, were put in place to give creditors additional remedy against a debtor. i. ATP 108:COMMERCIAL TRANSACTIONSTOPIC:TAKEOVERS AND ACQUISITIONSFIRM 8 CLASS C MEANING AND SCOPE A takeover according to Section 584 of the Companies Act is the making of an offer by a company to buy shares, whether the offer is to buy all the shares, a class of shares or all classes of shares of a company.The Black‚Äôs Law Dictionary (9th edition) define takeover as ‚Äòthe acquisition of ownership or control of a corporation.' A takeover is typically accomplished by a purchase of shares or assets, a tender offer, or a merger. Takeovers are a common business strategy aimed at consolidating market position, diversifying operations or entering new markets. MEANING AND SCOPE CONT'DAn acquisition on the other hand refers to an agreed, strategic, operation where one company acquires another. Acquisitions involve buying more than 50% of the target company stock to enable the acquiring company to make decisions without the approval of the target company, an example is in 2016 Longhorn publishers acquired 74% stake in Law Africa publishers.The acquiring company will be entitled to all the assets, liabilities, properties and equipment of the target company. Once acquisition is complete, the target company ceases to exist as it is absorbed by the acquirer.Businesses may engage in takeovers or acquisitions to increase their consumer bases, get access to new markets, acquire innovative technology or goods, or oust rivals. COMPANY LAW Mwangi J. Mbogo Content 1. Introduction 2. Types of companies 3. Formation of companies 4. Alteration of company status 5. Foreign companies 6. Execution of documents by companies 7. Introduction Definition of a company: ‚Ä¢ Sec 3 of the Companies Act 2015 defines a company as a company formed and registered under the Act or an existing company. ‚Ä¢ The term existing company is defined in sec 3 of the Act as a company formed and registered under the repealed companies act or other repealed ordinances as defined by the repealed companies act. Introduction ‚Ä¢ Buckley J. in Re: Stanley (1906) noted that the word company has no strict legal meaning and further noted that a company may be defined as an association of persons who contribute money or money‚Äôs worth to common stock and who employ it to a common purpose. ‚Ä¢ The above definition in Re: Stanley (1906) does not distinguish a company from a partnership. ‚Ä¢ Under common law, a company is considered a legal person or legal entity separate from and with perpetual succession. Historical background of companies ‚Ä¢ The company as a form of business organization was first applied by English merchants trading cloth and other goods overseas. ‚Ä¢ The company emerged during the mercantile era due to two reasons: ‚Ä¢ The need for large scale investment, production and operation to satisfy the market demand, as a result of which, traders were in dire need of a commercial de

DEFINITION Section 2 of the Companies Act defines a company as an association of people for a common object or objects. Thus unlike a Partnership which is an association of persons who come together for business with a view of profit, the objects of a company extend beyond profit making. COMPANIES AND PARTNERSHIPS A company is different from a partnership in that in a partnership, each partner is liable for the debts and liabilities of the firm without limits, except for limited partnerships. Second, a partnership has no separate existence from the partners; third, a partnership has no perpetual succession unlike a company, so that the death, retirement or bankruptcy of a partner ends it. Section 389 of the Companies Act provides that no association or partnership consisting of more than 20 persons may be formed for the purposes of carrying on any business for the acquisition of gains unless it is registered under the Companies Act, or is formed pursuant to some other statute. Section 2 of the Companies Act 2015 provides that a company is a company formed and registered under the Act or an existing company. It also provides that one natural person may incorporate a company. TYPES OF COMPANIES Companies may be statutory companies which are formed either by specific Acts of Parliament such as Kenya Ports Authority Act, Kenya Railways Corporation Act, etc. (statutory corporations), or State corporations (parastatals) which are formed under the State Corporations Act which gives 
... [Content truncated]

The constitution of the company ¬ß A company‚Äôs constitution consists primarily of the articles of association, and agreements and resolutions affecting the company‚Äôs constitution. ¬ß The Companies Act 2015 has significantly reduced the importance of the memorandum of association, and the articles now form the company‚Äôs principal constitutional document. ¬ß The constitution forms a statutory contract between the company and its members, and between the members themselves, but only those provisions relating to membership rights will constitute terms of the statutory contract. ¬ß A company that acts outside the scope of its objects clause will be acting ultra vires. ¬ß Companies incorporated under the Companies Act 2015 have unrestricted objects by default ‚Äì CA 2015 s28. ¬ß A company can alter its articles by passing a special resolution, although statute and the common law restrict a company‚Äôs ability to alter its articles. ¬ß Despite being the largest piece of legislation ever passed, the CA 2015 does not seek to exhaustively regulate the internal affairs of companies. Much is left to the companies themselves who will usually create their own internal rules via the company‚Äôs constitution. ¬ß A company‚Äôs constitution largely fulfils the same function as the constitution of a country, namely, to set out the powers, rights and obligations of those who are subject to the constitution. ¬ß Accordingly, a company‚Äôs constitution aims to set out the powers, rights and obligations of the company
... [Content truncated]

Consumer Protection in the Financial Service Sector in Kenya An evaluation of the treatment of consumer protection in the financial services sector through the provisions of the Central Bank of Kenya Act, the Banking Act, the Insurance Act, the Capital Markets Act and the Retirement Benefits Act and the Subsidiary legislations made thereunder and suggested reforms, if any, that may improve the situation. Table of Contents INTRODUCTION Consumer protection is about ensuring fair exchange between goods and service providers on the one hand and consumers on the other hand. A deliberate legal framework is necessary to counter-balance the inherent disadvantage of a consumer vis-a-vis the power, information and resources of their goods and service providers. As a result, reforms in the financial sector regulations center on the concept of consumer protection.3 From the onset, the Kenya 2010 Constitution entrenches the right to consumer protection, that is, consumers should be protected from unfair market practices from services and goods providers.4 This constitutional entrenchment, in conjunction with the Consumer Protection Act 2012 and sector-specific statutes, place enormous obligations and responsibilities on providers of financial services and goods (both private and public) to be discussed hereunder. With regard to the foregoing observation this paper purposes to evaluate the treatment of consumer protection in the financial services sector through the provisions of the Centr

Regulated vs Unregulated Products, & Cytonn Weekly #25/2021 Focus of the Week Following ongoing discussions in the financial markets around regulated and unregulated offers, a member of parliament recently requested the Chairperson of the Department Committee on Finance and Planning to issue a statement on unregulated / private offers. Therefore, in this week‚Äôs topical, our Research Team seeks to build on a previous focus on the same topic covered in 2020 and educate the general public about these two types of markets/investment categories, which are complementary and important to well-functioning financial markets. As such, we shall discuss the following: i. Introduction to Regulated and Unregulated markets, ii. Regulated and Unregulated Investment Categories, iii. Pros and Cons of Each Investment Category, iv. Issues to Consider for Each Category, v. Myths About Each Category, and, vi. Conclusion. Section I: Introduction to Regulated and Unregulated Investment markets Investment markets from a regulatory point of view can be divided into two large categories, regulated and unregulated markets. Regulated markets, often referred to as public markets are controlled by a regulatory body whose key mandate is to protect the public interest. The products in the public markets tend to be standardized and easily accessible to the public. Common examples of regulated products include shares purchased at the securities exchange, Fixed Deposits offered by Banks, Collective Investment S

The Facts B and L were directors of two companies, N and TPG. B and L indirectly held 35 per cent-of the issued ordinary shares in TPG, which in turn held 25.6 per cent of the issued ordinary shares in N. B and L induced the board of directors of N to purchase TPG's main assets, allegedly at an over-valuation. In accordance with Stock Exchange regulations, the consent of N's shareholders was obtained, but by an allegedly "tricky" circular. P, which held 3.2 per cent of N's issued ordinary shares, sued N, B, L and TPG claiming: (a) as representative of itselfand the other shareholders at the time of the action, equitable damages on behalf of the company for breach of the defendants' fiduciary duty to N ("the derivative claim"), and (b) on behalf of itself alone, common law damages for conspiracy against B and L ("the direct personal claim"). Later P sought to amend its originating process to claim: (c) on behalf of itself and the other shareholders at the time of the alleged conspiracy, (i) a declaration of entitlement to damages and (ii) damages ("the direct representative claim"). - 2. CONDITIONS, DISCRETIONARY AUTHORITY, AND DECLARATIONS CONDITIONS TO OBLIGATIONS Let‚Äôs continue with the Susan Seller/John Buyer house purchase hypothetical that we discussed in the last class. After negotiating the purchase price, John tells Susan that he needs to obtain a mortgage and that the application process will take about six weeks. He also tells her that while he is quite conÔ¨Ådent that he will obtain the mortgage, he does not want to be obligated to buy the house if he cannot obtain it. Susan agrees. To establish the mortgage as a contractual prerequisite to John‚Äôs obligation to buy the house, the purchase contract will use a condition. A condition is a state of facts that must exist before a party is obligated to perform. If that state of facts does not exist, the obligation to perform is not triggered. Uncertainty is a hallmark of a condition. For a state of facts to be a condition, those facts cannot be certain to occur. Thus, the passage of time cannot be a condition because it will occur. Conditions may appear in any type of agreement. They are not limited to acquisition agreements. A condition is not a condition to the making of a covenant. Instead, a condition is a state of facts that must exist before a party must perform the obligation that Ô¨Çows from a covenant. Many lawyers still refer to a condition as a condition precedent, although this usage is now no longer necessary. Previously, a condition was labeled either a condition precedent or a condition subse
... [Content truncated]

ATP 108 COMMERCIAL TRANSACTIONS 2024 TOPIC: COMMERCIAL CONTRACTS PART 1 ‚Äì INTRODUCTION TO ADVANCED CONTRACT DRAFTING 1.1 INTRODUCTION A well-drafted contract is elegant. Its language is clear and unambiguous, and its organization cohesive and thoughtful. But drafting a contract requires more than good writing and organizational skills. A drafter should have keen analytical skills; a superior ability to negotiate; a sophisticated understanding of business, the business deal, and the client‚Äôs business; a comprehensive knowledge of the law; and a discerning eye for details. 1.2 WHAT DOES A CONTRACT DO? A contract establishes the terms of the parties‚Äô relationship. It reÔ¨Çects their agreement as to the rules that will govern their transaction. The rules generally include ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß the statements of facts that each party made that induced the other to enter the transaction; each party‚Äôs promises as to its future performance; each party‚Äôs rights; the events that must occur before each party is obligated to perform; each party‚Äôs discretionary authority; how the contract will end, including the events that constitute breach and the remedies for breach; and the general policies that govern the parties‚Äô relationship. These rules are the parties‚Äô private laws, which the courts will enforce, subject to public policy exceptions found in statutes and common law. Data Protection Act In Kenya The Data Protection Act No. 24 of2019 (the Act or DPA hereinafter) which was enacted on 8th November 2019 has a commencement date of 25th November 2019. Its preamble states that it is meant to give effect to Article 31 (c) and (d) of the Constitution. These provisions guarantee that every person has the right to privacy, including not to have information relating to their family or private affairs unnecessarily required or revealed and not to have the privacy of their communications infringed. The Act is a clean-up of several bills that were introduced previously then withdrawn for one reason or the other. The new law comes against the backdrop of the European Union‚Äôs General Data Protection Regulations (EUGDPR). Most companies operating in Kenya were still grappling with compliance. Notably, the provisions of the Act mimic those of the EUGDPR and therefore companies that had taken steps towards compliance with EUGDPR will be a step ahead. Who is affected by the Data Protection Act? The Act limits its scope to data controllers or processors who are established and ordinarily reside in Kenya and process personal data of natural persons while in Kenya OR If they are not established or resident in Kenya, they process personal data of data subjects located in Kenya. This scope captures anyone whose operations involve collection or processing of personal information pertaining to Kenyan residents. For instance, any person running an app that collects p
... [Content truncated]

WWW.BRS.GO.KE GENERAL OVERVIEW OF THE DEBTOR‚ÄôS BANKRUPTCY PROCEEDINGS BY: JUDY MUGO, SENIOR ASSISTANT OFFICIAL RECEIVER What is bankruptcy? Bankruptcy is a legal proceeding initiated when a person is unable to repay their debts or obligations when they fall due. Bankruptcy offers an individual a chance to start afresh by pardoning debts that they declared at the time of entering into bankruptcy. In Kenya, bankruptcy proceedings are governed by the Insolvency Act 2015 and its Regulations. The High court is vested with the jurisdiction to hear and determine bankruptcy matters. The bankruptcy process Section 32 of the Act states that a debtor may make an application in court for a Bankruptcy Order adjudging him bankrupt. The legal process commences when the debtor files a Bankruptcy Petition in court. A Bankruptcy Petition can either be filed by the debtor in their own capacity or by the advocate representing the debtor. The debtor presents the following documents to the Official Receiver for approval and confirmation that he has complied with the requirements of the Act:Bankruptcy petition- Form No. 10 Supporting Affidavit- Form No.8 Application for bankruptcy trustee- Form No. 9 Statement of Affairs- Form 11 Once the Official Receiver confirms compliance, the debtor makes a statutory payment of Kshs. 30,000/= to the Official Receiver, who then issues a Certificate of Compliance in relation to the petition. The debtor proceeds to file all the aforementioned documents at the Hig

PRIMER ON DISTRIBUTION AGREEMENTS Scope This paper examines the relationship between a manufacturer or supplier and its distributor. It provides an overview of the principal legal and commercial considerations likely to apply when appointing a distributor. It explores the principal di=erences between the various types of distribution agreement (exclusive, non-exclusive, sole, selective). It also examines the di=erences between the appointment of an agent and distributor. A. Distribution and agency Distinction between distributors, resellers and agents It is important for a supplier or manufacturer, when considering how best to market, sell or distribute its products, to be aware of the di=erence, in legal and practical terms, between appointing an agent and a distributor. Agency and distribution compared Agent promotes contract between principal and customer Essentially, an agent is appointed by the principal to negotiate and possibly conclude contracts with customers on the principal‚Äôs behalf. Agents are paid commission on the sales they make, usually on a percentage basis. The only contract for sale of the products is made between the principal and the customer. The agent generally has no contractual liability to the customer. Essentials of a Maritime Insurance Contract Before discussing about essential elements of marine insurance, we have to know about marine insurance. A contract of marine insurance is such a contract, by which the insurer undertakes the risk as agreed provide financial support against marine losses. Therefore, we can say that, a contract of marine insurance extent there by agreed against marine wastes. That is to tell the wastes incident to marine adventure. There are some essential elements of marine insurance. Moreover, Marine insurance is a legal contract. For that without general elements, there are some essential elements of marine insurance. 1. Elements of general contract: In marine insurance contract, there are all elements of general insurance contract. Cause as an arrangement contract it a general contract. Which kinds of contract are including in general contract: (a)Two parties: In contract of marine insurance, there are two parties. One party is insurance company and another party is insurance holder. (b)Offer and acceptance: Like general contract, an insurance holder offered to insurance company for accept his policy. If a policy is accepted then contract are accomplish. (c)Legal Consideration: As natural contract, insurer makes confirmation to insurance holder for deducing risk by money. INSOLVENCY PROCEDURES CONTINUED Insolvency Procedures Continued Administration It involves taking in control by the administrator the whole or substantially all of the company's business and assets to manage the company's business and if need be to realize its assets for the purpose of paying one or more of its creditors. The administrator may also be appointed by the court to collect the protect the property of an insolvent company until its fate is determined by the court and to utilize the property or manage the company's affairs in discharging debt. The primary objective of the administration of a company is a) To maintain a company as a going concern b) Achieve a more favorable outcome for the company's group of creditors which would likely not be the case if the company were liquidated without first being under administration c) To realize the property of the company in order to make a distribution to one or more secured or preferential creditors The general rule is that the debt which an administration order is founded is due and payable or is undisputed. If the subject is disputed a petition for an administration order or liquidation would amount to ordinary proceedings for debt collection. The administration must not be used as a means of bringing pressure using the courts for payment of debts In the case of Re The Standard Limited Ex Parte Tricom Paper International BV [2002] KLR 644 it was held that if a petition is intended to enforce payment of a disputed debt it
... [Content truncated]

FINTECH REGULATION IN KENYA Introduction According to the Economic Survey Report of 2022[1], the value of output from the Information and Communication Technology (ICT) Sector rose by 6.9% from USD 4.03 billion in 2020 to USD 4.31 billion in 2021. Mobile money subscribers increased by 8.5% to 35.2 million in 2021 and the value of mobile commerce transactions increased by 63.2% to USD 116.4 billion in the same period. The Central Bank of Kenya (CBK) is the primary regulator, responsible for ensuring that all financial institutions operate within the set standards. Rationale for requirement Licenses serve as assurance that the Regulator has approved the DCP as a credible entity to conduct the digital credit business after fulfilling all requirements. Licensing requirement ensures that only those who are fit &amp; proper to provide credit are given the go ahead to do so. The requirement for licensing is aimed at protecting consumers from DCPs that have not been licensed. Licensed DCPs are regulated and it is assumed that they will comply with the law. This will ensure the safety &amp; safeguarding of interests of consumers. Requirement for consumer protectionReg 26- DCP shall ensure that any advertisement that it publishes does not include any false, misleading or deceptive representation &amp; this includes: (a) a representation that the provision of the credit has an approval, benefits or qualities that it does not in fact have; (b) a representation that the DCP has an approval, status, affiliation or connection that it does not in fact have; (c) an inaccurate or incomplete representation as to the interest rate, costs or charges payable under a digital credit facility.Reg 27- DCP shall not increase charges or limits or have a provision in the credit agreement that purports to vary credit terms without the customer‚Äôs consent. DCP shall not change its pricing model or parameters without the prior written approval of the Bank. DERIVATIVE ACTION INTRODUCTION AND DEFINITIONS A company is an artificial person capable of being injured and can, when wronged, institute court proceedings to protect its interests. Much as the law recognizes and appreciates the juristic personality of companies, one jurisprudential question that courts have to grapple with before proceeding to entertain suits seeking to remedy harm suffered by companies is: in whose name are such suits brought or rather, who is the proper plaintiff? In the case of Foss v Harbottle (discussed in detail later) it was held that the proper plaintiff in an action in respect of a wrong alleged to be done to a company is the company itself, which essentially mean the directors or majority shareholders must okay the lodging of such suits. The decision was termed oppressive as it denied members of a company, especially minority shareholders, the chance to prosecute matters where there is lethargy on the part of the directors or where the directors or majority shareholders themselves are the perpetrators of the injury suffered. Over time and in order to ensure proper management of companies and sustainability of their businesses, the law was changed to allow members of companies to initiate suits in their own names and on behalf of the company. This is known as derivative action. A derivative action has been variously defined. The Black‚Äôs Law Dictionary (8th edition) defines it as a suit brought by a beneficiary to enforce a right belonging to the fi
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

Forgery is the production of a replica to as close as possible to the actual object with the intention of deceiving people into accepting it as the original. Sec 345 of the Penal Code cap 63 defines forgery as the making of a false document with intent to defraud or to deceive Where the forgery consists of the drawer's signature and the banker pays such a cheque, he cannot normally debit his customer's account with the amount so paid out as he has not in actual fact received his customer's mandate to do so. Where the acceptor‚Äôs signature is forged, he incurs no liability, but all other persons whose signature are genuine are liable to the holder. Hence, the customer's signature on the cheque must be the same as the specimen. If it differs, the cheque should be returned "Drawer's signature differs". However, if the customer on discovering the forgery remains silent about it for an unreasonable time by the end of which the banker's ability to recover such payment becomes adversely affected, he is precluded from setting up the forgery and may have to suffer the loss himself: see Greenwood v. Martins Bank Ltd (1933). Similarly, In Kenya industrial Research and Development Institute v. National Bank of Kenya Civil Case No. 225 of 1995, it was held that a cheque on which a drawer‚Äôs signature is forged is a mere paper and is not a cheque at all unless it becomes valid by estoppel. An unauthorized signature is one not amounting to a forgery but one placed on an instrument by an agent

FRANCHISE &amp; DISTRIBUTORSHIP AGREEMENTS AGENCY VS DISTRIBUTIONDISTRIBUTIONThe supplier or manufacturer sells its products to the distributor, who then sells the products on to its customers, adding a margin to cover its own costs and profit. The distributor contracts both with the supplier and with its customer.Title to the products in question will pass to and from the distributor. the duties owed by the principal and agent to each other are replaced by mutual contractual rights and obligations within the distribution agreement.Less control by supplier, more risk assumed by distributor. AGENCY VS DISTRIBUTIONAGENCYAn agent is appointed by the principal to negotiate and possibly conclude contracts with customers on the principal's behalf. Agents are paid commission on the sales they make, usually on a percentage basis. The only contract for sale of the products is made between the principal and the customer. The agent generally has no contractual liability to the customer. Types of Distribution ArrangementsExclusive DistributionSupplier agrees to sell the contract products only to the distributor within a certain defined territory, and agrees not to appoint other distributors or sell the products directly to other customers within the territory.Sole Distribution Supplier appoints a distributor as its only or sole distributor within a territory, but the supplier reserves the right to supply the products directly to end users. Structure of Kenya‚Äôs Financial Sector APRIL 2023 ‚ö´The financial market deals with the purchase and sale of money. It therefore involves obtaining money from those who have more than they need by attracting it either as: ‚ö™ deposits through commercial banks; ‚ö™ premiums through insurance companies; ‚ö™ units or shares through Mutual Funds ‚ö™ or shares or bonds through investment banks. ‚ö´The money drawn from the savers is then pooled and allocated throughout the economy by giving it out as loans, common or corporate bonds, treasury bills inter alia. ‚ö´The financial market in Kenya can be said to have two major functions: ‚ö™ it allocates money capital through identifying those with surplus funds, attracting the funds into a pool and then distributing them to those who need to spend more than they have. ‚ö™ The other function of the financial market is the distribution of the economic risk through the creation and distribution of securities. ‚ö´Principally, financial market in Kenya comprise: ‚ö™ commercial banks, ‚ö™ non-bank financial institutions, ‚ö™ mortgage companies, ‚ö™ forex bureaus, ‚ö™ development finance institutions, ‚ö™ pension schemes, ‚ö™ SACCOs ‚ö™ the insurance sector and the stock market. Chapter 29 KENYA John Syekei, Philip Coulson and Babette M√§rzheuser-Wood 1 I INTRODUCTION The international franchise market in Kenya has grown extensively. There are currently a considerable number of international franchisors with established operations in the Kenyan market, for example KFC, Nando‚Äôs, Subway, Papa John‚Äôs and Dubai-based Baby Shop or South African Mr Price. Local franchisors are few but well established within the Kenyan market, such as the livestock services offered by Sidai Africa to local farmers and Sasini Tea and Coffee or Dormans Coffee. The Kenyan franchise industry is not formally organised into a national body but there is a Kenya Franchise Association (KEFRA) which was called into life as part of a private local initiative. II MARKET ENTRY i Restrictions There are no specific restrictions or approvals for foreign franchisors entering the Kenyan market. There are, however, regulatory issues relating to establishing a business or operating a branch in Kenya. Typically, it would be best to structure the franchise so that the franchisor does not acquire a local presence. Most franchise agreements are structured in this way. Kenyan law is based on common law principles and very similar to English law so that English law agreements can be used without substantial amendments. The decisions of the English courts are often used as persuasive precedents. Certain franchise agreements or arrangements may fall within the ambit of the Competition Act, 2010 if the
... [Content truncated]

FILE #179: General partnerships LLPs and limited partnerships comparison of key features.doc.docx

Full Path: /home/ngobiro/projects/pngobiro.github.io/colleges/ksl/Advocates Training Program/ATP 108 - Commercial Transactions/Notes/99_Miscellaneous/General partnerships LLPs and limited partnerships comparison of key features.doc.docx

General partnerships, LLPs and limited partnerships: comparison of key features This checklist sets out a comparison of the key features of general partnerships and limited partnerships under the Partnerships Act No. 16 of 2012, and limited liability partnerships (LLPs) incorporated under the Limited Liability Partnerships Act No. 42 of 2011 (Cap 30A). Scope of this checklist The table below provides a comparison of the key features of: General partnerships under the Partnerships Act 2012 (PA 2012). Many general partnerships (or ordinary partnerships as they are sometimes known) arise without the knowledge of the persons involved. Partnership is defined as the relation which exists between persons carrying on a business in common with a view to profit (section 3, PA 2012). It is a matter of fact whether a general partnership exists and the parties cannot simply determine this for themselves. Normally the relationship is governed by a contractual agreement but the essence of a partnership is the continuing relationship, personal as well as commercial, with the contractual agreement being only an indication of the relationship (Hurst v Bryk [2001] 1 AC 185). When the courts have to consider whether a general partnership exists, they look at the substance of the arrangements and not the stated intentions of the parties. One advantage a general partnership has over limited partnerships and LLPs is that they are simple and cheap to set up. No formal agreement is necessary as the t
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

General partnerships, Limited partnerships and LLPs General partnerships, LLPs and limited partnerships: comparison of key features This checklist sets out a comparison of the key features of general partnerships and limited partnerships under the Partnerships Act No. 16 of 2012, and limited liability partnerships (LLPs) incorporated under the Limited Liability Partnerships Act No. 42 of 2011 (Cap 30A). Scope of this checklist The table below provides a comparison of the key features of: ‚Ä¢ General partnerships under the Partnerships Act 2012 (PA 2012). Many general partnerships (or ordinary partnerships as they are sometimes known) arise without the knowledge of the persons involved. Partnership is defined as the relation which exists between persons carrying on a business in common with a view to profit (section 3, PA 2012). It is a matter of fact whether a general partnership exists and the parties cannot simply determine this for themselves. Normally the relationship is governed by a contractual agreement but the essence of a partnership is the continuing relationship, personal as well as commercial, with the contractual agreement being only an indication of the relationship (Hurst v Bryk [2001] 1 AC 185). When the courts have to consider whether a general partnership exists, they look at the substance of the arrangements and not the stated intentions of the parties. NEWS ALERT ISSUE: 17 JUNE 2017 2.0 Nature of rights over moveable properties covered by the Act (Security rights) A property right in a movable asset that is created by an agreement to secure payment or other performance of an obligation is defined as a security right under the Act. It is important to note that the Act adopts a ‚Äúsubstance over form‚Äù approach as the Act classifies any such right in any agreement as a security right regardless of whether the parties have denominated it as a security right. The Act also defines the right of the transferee in an outright transfer of a receivable as a security right. The Act applies to security rights in moveable assets, including: A BRIEF OUTLINE OF THE MOVABLE PROPERTY SECURITY RIGHTS ACT, 2017 1. Introduction The Moveable Property Security Rights Act, 2017 (the ‚ÄúAct‚Äù) came into operation on 16th May 2017. The Act aims at facilitating the use of movable property as security for credit facilities and enhancing the ability of individuals and entities to access credit using moveable assets. To this end, the Act creates the office of the Registrar of Security Rights and sets out provisions in respect of the registration of security rights in movable property, the realisation process and related matters. Areas of Law: How to Become a Commercial Lawyer How to Become a Commercial Lawyer: What is Commercial Law? Commercial law involves legal problems that can occur in the running of businesses and commercial transactions. Its predominant concerns are contract and tort law. Clients that come to commercial law firms include large businesses, governments, banks, insurance companies and more. Legal advice can be given to any part of the running of business, from offset (starting a business) all the way to dissolving a company. How to Become a Commercial Lawyer: Why is Commercial Law So Important? It is the area of law that is always thriving since it has a strong association with the economy, thus affecting society as a whole. Therefore, commercial law is incredibly important as it affects how businesses are run and how they in turn work to help and grow society. Furthermore, it provides the rules for businesses and organisations so as to ensure legal conduct avoiding fraudulent activity. How to Become a Commercial Lawyer: What does the World of Commercial Law Look Like? The top law firms in Kenya are often associated with commercial law. These are the most prestigious firms and are all commercial law firms. It is without a doubt a competitive area of law which many undergraduates hope to be a part of in their futures. Chapter One Sources of Law in Kenya 1.1 What is Law? The world of commerce and industry is founded on distinct pillars of social order. The establishment and regulation of the various institutions of society depend upon frameworks of rules to which they owe their existence and from which they draw their power and legitimacy. These rules are promulgated and enforced in accordance with a defined legal system. As has been rightly observed, ‚Äú[i]n order to ensure good order and the smooth running of society, it is necessary to formulate general rules which work well enough in the majority of cases.‚Äù1 Accordingly, there is need in every society to promulgate normative rules, principles and doctrines to regulate social order. Such rules and principles must be effectively binding in the sense that derogation from them, or breach thereof, attracts penal or other remedial consequences. The assemblage of these doctrines, principles, rules, and regulations, may, in very loose terms, be collectively referred to as law. Glanville Williams defines law as ‚Äú‚Ä¶ the body of principles recognized and applied by the state in the administration of justice ‚Ä¶‚Äù According to him, it consists of ‚Äú‚Ä¶ the rules recognized and acted on by courts of justice.‚Äù2 In this sense, law includes all rules of conduct enacted by Parliament or established by court decisions in conformity with fundamental principles and doctrines recognized as essential for the administration of justice. INSIGHTS / EXPERTISE / PEOPLE / NEWS / CONTACTS SITEMAP DISCLAIMER The Insolvency Act, 2015: The impact on creditors and their right to realise securities Posted on: December 5, 2015 The Insolvency Act, 2015(the Insolvency Act) was enacted with the key objective of amending and consolidating the legislation relating to the insolvency of natural persons and incorporated and unincorporated bodies. It aims to provide for and regulate the bankruptcy or liquidation of natural persons, incorporated and unincorporated bodies to enable their affairs to be managed for the benefit of their creditors. The Act was assented on 11th September, 2015 and will come into operation on such date as the Cabinet Secretary may direct by notice in the Kenya Gazette, however, any provision that is not brought into force through gazettement within nine months after the publication of the Act shall automatically come into force on the expiry of that period. Prior to the enactment of the Insolvency Act, corporate insolvency was dealt with under the Winding-up provisions of the Companies Act(Cap 486 of the Laws of Kenya) (the Companies Act) while the insolvency of natural persons was covered in the Bankruptcy Act of Kenya(Cap 53 of the Laws of Kenya) (the Bankruptcy Act). Unlike the previous legislation, the Act seeks to redeem insolvent companies through administration as opposed to liquidation. --- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
INSOLVENCY IN KENYA Historical Background For a long period of time the law governing insolvency in respect to individuals and companies in Kenya was the Bankruptcy Act, Cap 53 Laws of Kenya. This piece of legislation was enacted in 1938, and upon Kenya attaining its independence, like many other legislations in operation at the time, we adopted it and thus became the guide with which matters insolvency would be undertaken. Like many of the statutes we inherited from our colonial masters, this piece of legislation was a carbon copy version of the British Bankruptcy Laws. We adopted it and continued in its use over time despite the changing tides in this very area of our law to a point this law became more or less a moribund and backward piece of legislation. Due to concerted efforts exerted by various interest groups for a change in laws that affected the conduct of business within our jurisdiction, the Bankruptcy Act among many others such as the Companies Act came under a microscope and those concerned began the process of formulating a new law to govern matters insolvency. This desire is demonstrable from the several bills that have been debated within the precincts of parliament from the Insolvency Bill 2008, 2010, 2012 and finally the Insolvency Bill 2014 that was then assented to by the president on 11th September 2015 and is now known as the Insolvency Act No. 18 of 2015. It is important at this very onset to distinguish between the terms bankruptcy and insolvency. Ban
... [Content truncated]

INSOLVENCY PROCEDURES CONTINUATION There are two primary objects of bankruptcy. First is to vest all the property which the debtor has at the commencement of the bankruptcy or acquires before his discharge in a trustee for distribution amongst his creditors equitably according to their rights. What is a trust? A trust is a fiduciary relationship where a settlor (person setting up the trust) gives another party, known as the trustee, the right to hold title to property or assets for the benefit of a third party commonly referred to as beneficiaries. Types of trusts registrable in Kenya There are three types of trusts that can be incorporated in Kenya, namely; i. Family Trust A family trust is a trust, whether living or testamentary, partly charitable or non-charitable, that is registered or incorporated by any person or persons, whether jointly or as an individual, for the purposes of planning or managing their personal estate. ii. Charitable Trust A charitable trust is a trust formed for the exclusive purpose of the relief of poverty, the advancement of education, religion or human rights and fundamental freedoms, or the protection of the environment or any other purpose beneficial to the general public. iii. Non-charitable Trust A non-charitable is a trust created for a specific purpose, whether partly charitable or not, provided that the purpose/objects of the trust are not illegal. Process of incorporation of a trust Incorporation of a trust is done online through manual.brs.go.ke. The following persons can make an application for incorporation of a trust on behalf of the trust; i. ii. iii. iv.
