SPECIAL ISSUE - Kenya Gazette Supplement No.4 19 26th January,, 2018 (Legislative Supplement No.2) LEGAL NOTICE No.7 THE INSOLVENCY ACT (No. 18 of 2015) IN EXERCISE of the powers conferred by section 730 of the Insolvency Act, 2015, the Attorney General makes the following Regulations THE INSOLVENCY (AMENDMENT) REGULATIONS, 2018 1. These Regulations may be cited as the Insolvency (Amendment) Regulations, 2018. Citation. Regulation 4 of the Insolvency Regulations, 2016, in these Regulations referred to as "the principal Regulations" is amended in subregulation (2) (b) by deleting the expression "f" and substituting therefor the word "forms". Amendment of regulation 4 of L. N.No. I8of 2015. Regulation 5 of the principal Regulations is amended by deleting subregulation (4). Amendment of regulation 5 of L.N. No. 18 of 2015. Amendment of regulation 11 of L. N.No. 18 of 2015. In re James Maina Kabatha (Debtor/Applicant) [2020] eKLR REPUBLIC OF KENYA IN THE HIGH COURT OF KENYA AT NAKURU INSOLVENCY CAUSE NO. 4 OF 2019 IN THE MATTER OF JAMES MAINA KABATHA (DEBTOR/APPLICANT) RULING 1. The twin goals of consumer or individual bankruptcy law are to protect creditors and ensure optimal payment to them where possible; and the provision of shelter and a "fresh start" to individual debtors overburdened by debt. There is no doubt that these twin goals run through the Insolvency Act, 2015 and the Insolvency Regulations, 2016. 2. The US Supreme Court explained the fundamental goal of bankruptcy law in Local Loan Co. v. Hunt, 292 U.S. 234, 244 (1934) thus: [Bankruptcy law] gives to the honest but unfortunate debtor‚Ä¶a new opportunity in life and a clear field for future effort, unhampered by the pressure and discouragement of pre-existing debt. 3. The ‚Äúfresh start‚Äù goal is accomplished through the bankruptcy discharge, which usually releases the debtor from personal liability from certain debts and prevents creditors from taking any action against the debtor to collect those debts. 4. Consequently, bankruptcy protection being an extraordinary relief, one of the corollaries to the these seemingly conflictual twin goals of bankruptcy law ‚Äì the protection of creditors and the provision of fresh start for the honest but unfortunate debtor -- is that an individual seeking bankruptcy protection is required to scrupulously demonstrate that he is acting in good faith an

1. The Basics of Mergers and Acquisitions Over the past few decades, we have seen countless examples of companies, such as Amazon, Facebook, General Electric, Google, and Cisco, that have grown dramatically and built revenues through aggressive acquisition programs. Seasoned executives and entrepreneurs have always searched for efficient and profitable ways to increase revenues and gain market share. The typical strategic growth options are as follows: organic, inorganic, or by external means. Examples of organic growth are hiring additional salespeople, developing new products, and expanding geographically. The best example of inorganic growth is an acquisition of another firm, something that is often done to gain access to a new product line, customer segment, or geography. Finally, external revenue growth opportunities include franchising, licensing, joint ventures, strategic alliances, and the appointment of overseas distributors, which are available to growing companies as an alternative to mergers and acquisitions as a growth engine. Figure 1-1 discusses the benefits of organic versus other forms of growth. This book focuses primarily on mergers and acquisitions (M&A) as a means of growing, although toward the end of the book certain external means are explored as well. Figure 1-1. M&A Basics: Buy Versus Build At the heart of all decisions regarding mergers and acquisitions, regardless of the global financial crisis, is a fundamental question: Are we better off buying a

JOINT VENTURES ‚Äì A PRIMER 1. Introduction Joint ventures have become increasingly important as a medium through which companies undertake signiÔ¨Åcant business activities and projects. A major factor has been the escalating size of transactions and projects, which frequently require commitments on a scale beyond the resources of individual companies. Equally important, particularly in the cross-border context, are changing business conditions and markets (largely as a result of changes in political and economic conditions, deregulation and technological developments) which provide opportunities for companies to enter into joint ventures as part of their strategic planning in relation to particular industries or markets. The rationale and perceived advantages of particular joint ventures (both large and small) vary from case to case. Whatever the advantages for the individual participant, however, a joint venture inevitably involves a sacriÔ¨Åce of the control and Ô¨Çexibility which it might otherwise have enjoyed had it undertaken a business or project independently, and exposure to the tensions that are an inherent part of a joint venture relationship. Against this background, the challenge for lawyers involved in planning and documenting a joint venture is to help create a structure that not only meets the technical, operational, Ô¨Ånancial, accounting, regulatory, legal and tax requirements of the venture but also encapsulates the underlying commercial objectives of the individual
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

Transfer of Personal Data Outside Kenya The Data Protection Bill that has been a subject of discussion for years, was passed into law on 8 November 2019 when the president assented to it. Key Franchising Considerations Introduction Franchising is a contractual arrangement by which franchisors, in exchange for an upfront payment or a series of payments (usually both), grant franchisees (usually independently owned and Ô¨Ånanced) the right to operate a business and sell or distribute goods or services, or goods and services, identiÔ¨Åed with the franchisor‚Äôs trademarks, trade names, and other intellectual property. Franchisors typically exercise signiÔ¨Åcant control over the format and quality standards of franchisee businesses by requiring them to operate according to the franchisor‚Äôs requirements, set out in one or more contractually binding franchise manuals. Franchisors normally take care not to be involved in the management of the businesses of the franchisees so that the business risk is with the franchisees, but they usually provide support to assist franchisees in complying with the franchise agreement and franchise manuals. Franchising can have many beneÔ¨Åts for a foreign supplier, including the ability to expand and capture new markets quickly with limited capital expenditure. However, although franchising is lightly regulated in Kenya, counsel advising prospective franchisors planning to establish a franchising arrangement should consider the risks and requirements that may arise. This paper considers the following aspects of franchising: ¬ß Key legal and regulatory requirements governing franchising arrangements, including: ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ ‚Ä¢ absence of a statutory
... [Content truncated]

MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS &amp; TAKEOVERS MERGERS A merger is a voluntary amalgamation of two firms on roughly equal terms into one new legal entity. The decision is usually mutual between the merging companies. Mergers are mostly geared towards enjoying greater economies of scale and increasing a companies competitiveness in the market. The term ‚Äúmerger‚Äù is defined in Section 2 of the Competition Act, 2010 (the Act) as ‚Äúany acquisition of shares, business or other assets, whether inside or outside of Kenya, resulting in the change of control of a business, part of a business or an asset of a business in Kenya, in any manner and includes a takeover. Sec 41 of the Act goes ahead to define mergers in greater details to includea) the purchase or lease of shares, acquisition of an interest, or purchase of assets of the other undertaking in question;(b) the acquisition of a controlling interest in a section of the business of an undertaking capable of itself being operated independentlyThe Capital Markets (Take-overs and mergers) Regulations, 2002 provides that a merger is an arrangement whereby the assets of two or more companies become vested in or under the control of one company6/8/202214WAKIMANI 6/8/20227WAKIMANI 6/8/2022WAKIMANI6 6/8/2022WAKIMANI5 6/8/20224WAKIMANILINK POISON PILLPoison pills allow existing shareholders the right to purchase additional shares at a discount, effectively diluting the ownership interest of a new, hostile party. EFFECTING CORPORATE RECONSTRUCTIONAlter the object clause to incorporate a new business after reconstruction. The objects can be changed or amended by a special (or written) resolution of the company. The resolution altering the memorandum must be filed with the Registrar of Companies within fourteen (14) days, and if no application is made to the Court, the company must within fourteen (14) days from the end of the period for making an application, deliver to the Registrar of Companies a printed copy of the memorandum as altered ( See companies Act section 8) Banking actWhen a resolution is passed, a notice confirming the amalgamation or transfer of assets and liabilities, a copy of the resolution together with the terms and conditions must be sent to the Central Bank by each institution. It is vital to note that the said copies must be certified by the chairperson and the secretary. When the Central Bank receives the notices, it is supposed to publish them and after that issue a license to the new institutionThe Act under Section 9(9) waives payment of transfer fees, stamp duty, registration fees, license duty or other charges on a transfer of assets and liabilities in a merger of a licensed financial institution. Law of Limited liability Partnerships What is an LLP s6 - An LLP is not a partnership; rather it is a body corporate registered under the Limited Liability Partnership Act, No 42 of 2011 (the ‚ÄúAct‚Äù). On being registered under the Act, an LLP becomes a body corporate with perpetual succession with a legal personality separate from that of its partners or members. The idea of LLPs came about in the UK in the 1990s when some of the larger professional services partnerships became increasingly concerned by the risk of substantial personal liability of their partners, and applied pressure on government to address the issue. The consequence of this pressure was the entity called LLP, which retains the internal flexibility of a partnership but carries the all important limitation of liability for the partners. The law applicable to LLPs The main law applicable to LLPs is the Limited Liability Partnership Act, No 42 of 2011. s8 - The Partnership Act shall apply to an LLP except so far as a provision of the Act expressly provides otherwise. Like a company, an LLP must be formed by application to and registered at the companies registry. The Registrar of Companies is the Registrar of LLPs, and she may depute a deputy or an assistant registrar to exercise her functions under the Act. A body corporate Unlike a general or limited partnership, an LLP is a body corporate with unlimited capacity and has a legal capacity separate from its members. All this means that like a company: It can su
... [Content truncated]

--- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
1. Business structures ¬ß The four principal business structures are the sole proprietorship, the partnership, the limited liability partnership, and the company. ¬ß Sole proprietorships are the most common business structure and involve little formality, but the liability of the sole proprietor is personal and unlimited. ¬ß Two or more persons who wish to conduct business can form an ordinary partnership. Such partnerships are subject to greater regulation than sole proprietorships, but less regulation than limited liability partnerships and companies. The liability of the partners is personal and unlimited. ¬ß Limited liability partnerships were created largely to be a suitable business vehicle for large professional firms. In many respects, limited liability partnerships closely resemble companies. ¬ß Public companies are so called because they can offer to sell their shares to the public at large. Private companies cannot offer to sell their shares to the public at large. There are other notable differences between public and private companies. ¬ß The vast majority of companies are limited companies, and so their members will usually have limited liability. Introduction A person who wishes to engage in some form of business activity will need to do so via some form of business structure, with differing business structures providing different advantages and disadvantages. DIGITAL/ MOBILE LENDING Modern day lending is moving towards digital lending and away from Banks with emergence of mobile financial services. Digital technology is transforming the consumer loan industry by providing borrowers with greater simplicity, efficiency, and transparency. The lending process refers to the sequence of activities an FSP (Financial Service Provider) performs to provide credit from acquiring and onboarding a customer, evaluating the customer and disbursing the loan, receiving repayments and following up on past-due loans. Throughout the lending process, the FSP builds customer engagement and loyalty through high-touch interactions that adapt to client needs and preferences. The FSP‚Äôs lending involves the digital channels for acquisition, disbursement, repayment, and engagement and by leveraging digitized data and advanced algorithms for credit decisions, collections, and customer engagement. Digital lenders use digital data from different business functions and build the Data Bank to make quicker, automated, and more accurate underwriting decisions using both conventional and alternative data sources and advanced algorithms and analytics to quickly and remotely ‚Äòscore‚Äô potential clients and make credit decisions. FIRM 18 - CLASS E COMMERCIAL TRANSACTIONS PART 7 Liquidation of an Unregistered Companies What is an unregistered company? Unregistered company includes any association and any company other than a company registered under the Companies Act, 2015. Companies registered under the Companies Act, 2015 are private and public limited or unlimited companies. All other entities are therefore considered unregistered companies for purposes of Insolvency Act, 2015. Circumstances in which unregistered company can be liquidated Liquidation is neither defined under the Insolvency Act, 2015 nor the Companies Act, 2015. The Black‚Äôs Law Dictionary defines ‚Äòliquidate‚Äô as to settle an obligation by payment or to determine the liabilities and distribute the assets of an entity especially in bankruptcy or dissolution. Liquidation of an unregistered company is a new concept introduced in the new Insolvency Act, 2015. An unregistered company can be liquidated like any other registered company under Part VI of the Insolvency Act, 2015. This part provides for liquidation of registered companies. Under this part, liquidation may be either: Voluntary ‚Äì which can either be; Members‚Äô Voluntary liquidation ‚Äì where a resolution has been passed by members for voluntary liquidation and the directors have sworn a statutory declaration as to the ability of the company to pay its debts. Creditors‚Äô Voluntary liquidation - where no statutory declaration has been made as above to assure creditors of payment of the

University of London Press Ltd v University Tutorial Press Ltd [1916] 2 Ch 601 Court: Chancery Division Judges: Peterson J Date decided: 26 July 1916 Examiners were hired to create exam scripts for the University of London. Peterson J held that the papers were original literary works: Assuming that they are ``literary work'‚Äô, the question then is whether they are original. The word ``original'‚Äô does not in this connection mean that the work must be the expression of original or inventive thought. Copyright Acts are not concerned with the originality of ideas, but with the expression of thought, and, in the case of ``literary work'‚Äô, with the expression of thought in print or writing. The originality which is required relates to the expression of the thought. But the Act does not require that the expression must be in an original or novel form, but that the work must not be copied from another work ‚Äî that it should originate from the author. In the present case it was not suggested that any of the papers were copied. Professor Lodge and Mr Jackson proved that they had thought out the questions which they set, and that they made notes or memoranda for future questions and drew on those notes for the purposes of the questions which they set. The papers which they prepared originated from themselves, and were, within the meaning of the Act, original. It was said, however, that they drew upon the stock of knowledge common to mathematicians, and that the time spent in producing the
... [Content truncated]

How does the mergers and acquisitions (M&A) process start? The process is more informatively looked at from the Target‚Äôs point of view. The Target may decide to put itself up for sale because it needs to for Ô¨Ånancial reasons, because it thinks it has reached its peak value, or because one or more shareholders need or want liquidity; or it may have been approached by a potential Buyer (often a customer or supplier) who wants to talk about ‚Äúmatter of mutual interest.‚Äù Before the Target puts itself up for sale, it is likely that its corporate and legal aDairs will have to be cleaned up. The Target‚Äôs lawyers may or may not have had close contact and involvement with their client during the course of their engagement. M&A IN AFRICA Current Trends, 2023 www.bowmanslaw.com 1 INTRODUCTION In this, our annual Africa M&A roundup, we discuss in brief the developing trends from 2023, followed by a targeted review of select jurisdictional developments. M&A in Africa has not been immune to global challenges, but remains resilient. There have been notable developments in the fintech and renewable energy spaces respectively and an increase in demand for African exports as the global demand for essential minerals continues to rise. The African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA) and related agreements and protocols are gaining traction, already growing regional value chains and enhancing collaboration and capabilities. African deal metrics have remained largely constant. There has been an uptick in cross-border deals and restructurings, an emerging trend of joint ventures and the re-emergence of auction sales. Across Africa, exchanges are cutting red tape and embarking on simplification projects in an effort to increase listings amid the uptick in take-privates and unbundlings. Regulatory, public interest and local ownership considerations and shareholder activism are playing an increasing role in deal structuring across Africa. So too are in-country risk factors. Highly negotiated clauses include limitation of liability provisions, security, conditionality, purchase price mechanisms, material adverse change and force majeure clauses. MATERIAL ALTERATION IN THE BILL OF EXCHANGE By Sharon K The following alterations will not vitiate an instrument: (i) An alteration, which, though made in a material part, was made before the instrument was issued. (ii) An alteration made for the purpose of correcting a mistake, such as the correction of mistake in respect of date on a bill e.g. if the date is wrongly written i.e 13/09/2006 instead of 13/09/2016.Material alteration cont.. Non-Apparent alteration / Clever alteration The holder is given further rights. Not only can he hold D and E liable for the full $80 as before but he can also hold A, B, &amp; X liable for $ 8 (original tenor) Therefore, B would claim his $ 80 from E and E similarly from D. D would be able to claim $ 80 from C if he could be found, but failing that $8 from A,B,X, thereby losing $72 by C‚Äôs authorized alteration.Material alteration cont.. Apparent alteration F will look at C and subsequent endorsers, D and E for the $80 he gave for the bill. But he has no rights to against those parties who did not make, assent to or authorize the alteration.Material alteration cont.. SCENARIO A draws a bill for $8 payable to B. It is accepted by X, and B negotiates it by special endorsement to C. C alters the Bill to $80 and persuades D to give him $80 for it. The Bill by special endorsements passes from D to E then to F. F presents it for payments on the due date but X refuses payment because of the alteration. What is the position of F?Material alteration c

Arowwai Industries MERGERS AND ACQUISITIONS WELCOME TO OUR PRESENTATION TERMS OF REFERENCE: The mergers and acquisitions process has many oversight bodies. This came at the expense of merging companies‚Äô organisations. Discuss this in light of the various laws that govern mergers. Arowwai Industries MERGER AND ACQUISITION DEFINITION Capital Markets (Mergers and Takeovers) Regulations 2002 An arrangement whereby the assets of two or more companies become vested in or under the control of one company. Background Protecting minority shareholders' rights is not a common practice in takeovers. The laws and market practices have often protected the majority shareholders at the expense of minority shareholders. In Kenya, the law has been no more than unstable regarding the protection of minority shareholders' rights. Before 2019, the Companies Act 2015 provided for compulsory acquisition rights (squeeze-in) , allowing the acquiring company to "buy out" the dissenting minority shareholders. This is similar to the "drag along" principle in the repealed Companies Act. The sell-out rights were two parts: the acquiring company must acquire or unconditionally contract to acquire 90% of the shares relating to the offer and 90% of the voting rights in the target company. The law also provided for sell-out rights , which allowed the acquiring company to buy out the minority shareholders by purchasing their shares at the offer price, thus allowing the minority shareholders to exit the deal. However, the Statute Law (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act (Act No. 12 of 2019) (Amendment Act) introduced an amendment to the 2015 Act by lowering the threshold from 90% to 50%. The lowered threshold allowed the acquiring company to force out the non-assenting shareholders effortlessly if 50% of the issued shares accepted the offer. This change received a backlash as it undermined the minority shareholders' rights to decline a hostile takeover offer. This law was again changed in 2019, raising the thres
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

TTHE MOVEABLE PROPERTY SECURITIES ACT 2017 PURPOSE OF THE ACT The Memorandum of Objects and Reasons in the bill proposing this law describes the intention of this law as follows:- i) to provide for the use of movable property as collateral for credit facilities; ii) to establish the office of the Registrar of security rights; iii) to promote consistency and certainty in secured financing relating to movable assets; iv) to enhance the ability of individuals and entities to access credit using movable assets; and v) to establish a Registry to facilitate the registration of notices relating to security rights in movable assets. SCOPE OF THE ACT The Act applies to security rights in movable assets including every transaction that secures payment or performance of an obligation, a chattel mortgage, credit purchase transaction, credit sale agreement, floating and fixed charge, pledge, trust indenture, trust receipt and financial lease (a lease under which at the end the lessee automatically becomes the owner of the asset that is the object of the lease or, may acquire ownership of the asset by paying no more than a nominal price or, the asset has no more than a nominal residual value). However, the Act does not apply to security rights in proceeds of collateral if the proceeds constitute a type of asset that is governed by another law. The Act also does not apply to transactions specified under section 4(2) which include, among others, a security right in book entry securities unde
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

The Movable Property Security Rights Act, 2017 (‚Äúthe Act‚Äù) was assented into law on 10th May, 2017.This new law will facilitate the use of movable property as collateral for credit facilities, establish the office of the Registrar of Security Rights and provide for the registration of security rights in movable property. This Act will also benefit small and medium-sized enterprises which experience difficulty in accessing finance from the formal sector. Overview The Act has repealed the Chattels Transfer Act (Cap. 28) and the Pawnbrokers Act (Cap. 529.It has amended several sections of the Agricultural Finance Corporation Act (Cap. 323), the Stamp Duty Act (Cap. 480), the Hire Purchase Act (Cap. 507),), the Business Registration Services Act (Act No. 15 of 2015), the Companies Act, 2015 and the Insolvency Act, 2015. The Objects of the Act and certainty in secured financing relating to movable assets. Significant Provisions of the Act It is important to note that the Act, has brought with it significant provisions. These provisions are: a) Creation of a Security Right b) Types of Securities Created The Act assists persons who do not own real (immovable) property to secure credit by facilitating borrowing against their various types of movable assets. The Act provides that a security right can encumber the following:- i. any type of movable asset, whether tangible or intangible, including future assets (a moveable asset which does not exist or which the grantor does not have ri
... [Content truncated]

GENERAL OVERVIEW OF THE MOVABLE SECURITY RIGHTS ACT 2017 PRESENTED BY STEPHEN MALLOWAH, PARTNER, CORPORATE & COMMERCIAL OUTLINE SCOPE OF THE ACT PURPOSE OF THE ACT SIGNIFICANT PROVISIONS OF THE ACT CONCLUSION SCOPE OF THE ACT ‚Ä¢ The Act applies to security rights in movable assets. The Act repealed the Chattels Transfer Act (Cap 28). Movable assets include any tangible or intangible assets. Examples of tangible assets include motor vehicles, machinery, crops and livestock while examples of intangible assets include intellectual property rights and receivables owed to a business considered as assets. ‚Ä¢ The Act also applies to chattel mortgage and transactions that secure payment or performance of an obligation which is limited to transactions not specified under Section 4(2) of the Act. SCOPE CONT‚ÄôD ‚Ä¢ According to Section 2 of the Act, security rights means a property right in movable assets that is created by an agreement to secure payment or performance of an obligation regardless of whether the parties have denominated it as a security right and regardless of the type of asset, the status of the grantor or secured creditor or the nature of the secured obligation. ‚Ä¢ It is also prudent to note that the Act does not provide for security rights of assets that are governed by another law. The objects, as per Section 3, describe the intentions of this Act are to: PURPOSE OF THE ACT 1. Provide secured financing relating to movable assets. 2. Enhance the ability of individuals and e
... [Content truncated]

MPSRA OBJECTS OF ACT The preamble of the Act provides that, the Act was passed so as to; 1)to enable the use of movable property as security for credit facility 2)to establish the office of the registrar of security rights 3)to provide for the registration of security rights in movable property Definition of terms; Collateral-a movable asset that is subject to a security right or a receivable that is subject to an outright transfer Movable property -these are tangible assets like vehicles, crops, livestock, machinery and intangible assets like; - receivables (amount owed to business) -chores in action (right to sue for damages for an injury, right of a beneficiary to an estate of a deceased and rights of an employee to unpaid wages) -deposit accounts -electronic security (securities not represented by a certificate) - IP rights The MPSR Act 2017 provides that for steps of creating a security right in a movable property and making it enforceable; 1)Creation- this is done through a security agreement - it enables the rights of the grantor in the property to be transferred to the secured creditor 2)Perfection- this is when a third party is informed of the existence of the security through Registering the security interest with the government 3) Enforcement-recover the amount borrowed by realizing the value of the movable property. eg. by sale Section 3 of the MPSR provides that the Act has an objective of; 1)promote consistency and certainty in securing finances using movable pr

COMPETITION AUTHORITY OF KENYA MERGER NOTIFICATION FORM CAK/M&A/F-001 Instructions 1. This form is issued in terms of Section 43 (1) of the Competition Act which stipulates that each of the undertakings to the merger shall notify the Authority of the proposal in writing or in the prescribed manner. 2. The objective of this merger notification form is to improve the efficiency, transparency and predictability of the merger notification process. 3. Please note that the Competition Act empowers the Competition Authority of Kenya to obtain information relevant to evaluate mergers and acquisitions. This form facilitates the process by identifying relevant information depending on the activities of the merging parties and whether they are operating in the same markets and/or are actual or potential customers or suppliers to each other. By anticipating the likely information required by CAK and collating it in advance the time taken for the evaluation of the merger will be reduced. However, omitting relevant information may lead to the merger evaluation being delayed. This table below summarizes the schedules which should be normally submitted, depending on the characteristics of the merger. The thresholds referred to therein are according to the CAK merger threshold guidelines. Mergers &amp; AcquisitionsGENERAL Term Sheets, Exclusivity &amp; Confidentiality AgreementsHeads of terms (also known as a letter of intent, heads of agreement or memorandum of understanding) is not intended to be legally binding, except for specifically identified provisions relating to exclusivity of negotiations, transaction costs and governing law. It assumes that the parties have entered into a separate confidentiality agreement. Legal due diligenceThe primary purpose of the due diligence review is to obtain sufficient information about the target's business to enable the buyer to decide whether the proposed acquisition represents a sound commercial investment. It is effectively an audit of the target's affairs - legal, business and financial. THE COMPETITION AUTHORITY OF KENYAThe Competition Authority of Kenya (CAK) is established by the Competition Act, 2010 (the Act). Its mandate is to promote and safeguard competition in the national economy. The Mergers and Acquisitions Department analyzes notified merger applications and, either, approves the transaction with or without conditions or rejects it.The Department also investigates all mergers that may have been implemented without the Authority‚Äôs approval and gives recommendations, it also identifies and analyses unwarranted concentration of economic power.Section 42 (2) of the Act requires that merging entities should seek the approval of the Authority prior to implementing a merger where such merger would lead to the acquisition of control over an undertaking. THE PAYMENT SYSTEMS LEGAL COMPONENTS.Class B, Firm 3 Introduction to payment systems.What is a payment system?A payment system is any system used to settle financial transactions through transfer of monetary value.Section 4A(2) of the Central Bank of Kenya Act defines payment system as a system of instruments, procedures and rules for the transfer of funds among system participants.It is also the process through which a debtor discharges indebtedness to a creditor. Cont.This system settles financial transactions through the transfer of monetary value, and includes the institutions, instruments, people, rules, procedures, standards and technologies that make such exchange possible.Participants of the system include the CBK, the government, commercial banks, financial institutions and payment service providers.NPS are classified into two categories:Large valueLow value The Legal Framework.The Constitution of Kenya, 2010.Article 231 outlines the principles of public finance and establishes the CBK. Article 232 establishes the office of the Controller of budget who monitors the NPS to ensure the payments made through the system are consistent with the national budget.Article 33 provides for the right to privacy. NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS INTRODUCTION There are certain documents, which are freely used in commercial transactions and monetary dealings. These documents, if they satisfy certain conditions, are known as negotiable instruments. ‚ÄòNegotiable‚Äô is a word to mean transferable from one person to another in return for consideration. ‚ÄòInstrument‚Äô means a written document by which right is created in favor of some person. The law relating to negotiable instrument is contained in the Bill of Exchange Act (Cap. 27) and the Cheque Act (Cap 41), which deals with promissory notes, bills of exchange and cheques. As per the Act, no definition is given. However, in simple, a negotiable instrument can be described as a commercial document which represents money. It passes to a bonafide transferee free from any defect. Therefore a negotiable instrument refers to a document, which entitles a person to a sum of money or money value which is transferable from one person to another by mere delivery or by endorsement and delivery. NATURE OF NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS A negotiable instrument is a Chose in Action. A chose in action is a property right which cannot be enjoyed by physical possession, but which can only be enforced by legal action, for Example a debt, patent or copyright etc. Most types of chose in action are assignable but; 1. Notice to the debtor is always advisable and sometimes essential; and 2. The assignee can obtain no better rights than the assignor. 3. However, out of customary usage
... [Content truncated]

FINANCIAL SERVICES REGULATION National Payment System A payments System refers to a system or arrangement that enables payments to be effected between a payer and a beneficiary, or facilitates the circulation of money, and includes any instruments and procedures that relate to the system. A payment system ‚Äúconsists of a set of instruments, banking procedures and, typically, interbank funds transfer systems that ensure the circulation of money.‚Äù They are a major channel by which money can be transmitted across domestic and international financial systems and markets. Payment infrastructure refers to the system of bank branches, ATMs and point of sale (POS) systems, along with bank and non-bank agents providing banking or payment or mobile money services to customers in areas which are often not served by branches or ATMs.‚Äô Therefore, National Payments System are the conduits through which buyers and sellers of financial products and services make transactions and are an important component of a country‚Äôs financial system. In Kenya participants comprise of: The Government, Central Bank of Kenya, Commercial Banks, Financial Institutions Payment System Providers (PSPs): Include commercial banks, Micro-Finance Institutions, SACCOs, mobile payment service providers and international money remittance providers Clearing participants: Both bank and non-bank institutions that exchange payment instructions between PSPs. (a) Title passes by delivery: The property in a negotiable instrument passes from one person to another by ‚Äì (i) delivery, alone, if the instrument is payable to bearer, and (ii) endorsement plus delivery, if it is payable to order. NB: A legal assignment of an ordinary chose in action must, on the other hand, be in writing. Negotiable Instruments in Kenya by RauttaAthiambo [2014] 2 Characteristics ‚Ä¶ contd. (b) Holder gets free from defects: A person taking an instrument bona fide and for value, known as a holder in due course, gets the instrument free from all defects in the title of the transferor. NB/Contrast: An assignee of an ordinary chose in action gets no better title than his assignor had. (c) Holder can sue in own right: The holder in due course can sue upon a negotiable instrument in his own name for the recovery of the amount. Negotiable Instruments in Kenya by RauttaAthiambo [2014] 3 Characteristics ‚Ä¶.contd. (d) No notice to debtor: Further the holder need not give notice of transfer to the party liable on the instrument to pay, i.e. the debtor or other obligee. DEFINITION Section 3.(1) defines it as ‚ÄúPartnership is the relation which subsists between persons carrying on a business in common with a view of profit.‚Äù TYPES OF PARTNERSHIPS Under Kenyan Law there are 2 types of partnerships:- General Partnerships (the Partnerships Act, Cap.29) Limited Partnerships (Limited Partnerships Act, Cap.30) CHARACTERISTICS OF PARTNERSHIP Membership: It consists of 2 - 20 members Personality: It is an unincorporated association Utmost good faith: It is a contract uberimae fidei Agency: Each partner is agent of every other and the firm. Sue or be sued: It can sue or be sued in its registered name Profit-Motivated: A partnership is a profit-motivated enterprise. Liability: Partners liability for debts and other obligations of the firm is generally unlimited. Death, bankrupt or insanity may lead to dissolution. FORMATION OF PARTNERSHIP the formation of a partnership is not subject to any legal formalities, the agreement between the parties may be:- Oral or by word of mouth. Written with or without seal Implication from conduct of the parties. As was the case in Mohammed v. Hussein. However, the partners may on their own accord reduce the basis of their relationship into a formal document detailing the terms and the condition of the association. The document is the Partnership Deed or Agreement or Articles of Partnership. It is not a legal requirement. CONTENTS OF THE PARTNERSHIP DEED Nature of business Contribution of the partners. PN/01: Incorporation of Companies This publication is not a legal document. It contains general information intended as a basic guide on the main requirements for the incorporation of a limited company under the Companies Act 2015 (‚ÄúCompanies Act‚Äù) and searching and reservation of company names. This guide should not be deemed to substitute any need to take specialist professional advice. This guide will be relevant to you if you want to incorporate a company. These Practice Notes were prepared by the Joint Liaison Committee (‚ÄúJLC‚Äù) comprisedof members from Business Registration Service, Law Society of Kenya ‚Äì Nairobi Branchand Institute of Certified Secretaries. CHAPTER 1: HOW TO INCORPORATE A COMPANY What is incorporation? Incorporation is the process by which a new or existing business registers as a company.A business cannot be registered as a company unless it has been incorporated and registered at the Companies Registry under the Companies Act, 2015. Who can incorporate a company? One or more legal persons (which includes individuals, companies, government owned entities, non-profit organizations and other legal persons excluding an unincorporated Trust) can form a company by subscribing their names to a memorandum of association. Which types of companies are available? There are five types of companies: Private company limited by shares: This type of company has share capital and theliability of each member is limited to the amount, if any, unpaid on their shares. PN/02: Statutory Books and Records This publication is not a legal document. It contains general information intended as a basic guide on statutory books and records required of a limited company under the Companies Act, 2015. This guide should not be deemed to substitute any need to take specialist professional advice. Once registered a company must maintain the books and records outlined below in order to be compliant with the requirements of the Companies Act and Regulations. These Practice Notes were prepared by the Joint Liaison Committee (‚ÄúJLC‚Äù) comprised of members from Business Registration Service, Law Society of Kenya ‚Äì Nairobi Branch and Institute of Certified Secretaries. PART A - COMPANY REGISTERS Register of members Under Section 93 of the Companies Act, a company should keep a register of members.The register of members must contain an entry for each member of the company showing: The member's name and address; The date on which the person was registered as a member; The date on which any person ceased to be a member; and Where the company has a share capital: the number and class of shares held by each member (and, if the shares arenumbered, their distinguishing numbers); and the amount paid or agreed to be considered as paid on the member's shares. The register of members must be lodged with the Registrar of Companies within 30 days of its preparation. Any amendment made to the register of members should also be lodged with the Registrar of Companies within 1

PN/08: Specific compliance obligations for quoted/listed companies This publication is not a legal document. It contains general information intended as a basic guide on specific compliance obligations for Quoted Companies/Listed Companies underthe Companies Act 2015. This guide should not be deemed to substitute any need to takespecialist professional advice. This guide will be relevant to quoted/listed companies. These Practice Notes were prepared by the Joint Liaison Committee (‚ÄúJLC‚Äù) comprised of members from Business Registration Service, Law Society of Kenya ‚Äì Nairobi Branch andInstitute of Certified Secretaries. Director‚Äôs remuneration report? Under section 659, a Director‚Äôs remuneration report is a report that tabulates fees and emoluments earned by Directors of Quoted Companies in any given financialyear. How often should a Director‚Äôs remuneration report be prepared? A Director‚Äôs remuneration report is to be prepared for each financial year. What are the Contents of a Director‚Äôs remuneration report? The Law of Partnerships Mwangi J Mbogo ATP 108 ‚Äì Commercial Transactions May 2018 Definition of partnership s2 ‚Äì ‚Äúpartnership‚Äù means the relationship which exists between persons who carry on business in common with a view to making a profit. A partnership is not a distinct legal entity; rather it is a relationship between persons. The key element of the relationship between partners is that they are carrying on business, in common, with a view to making profit. ‚ÄúBusiness‚Äù includes any trade, profession or occupation. The objects of the partnership must be lawful, otherwise the partnership will be deemed illegal and liable to dissolution. See Foster v Driscoll [1929] 1 KB 70. The Act in s3 excludes certain bodies from consideration as partnerships: Limited liability partnerships Bodies corporate; or Foreign limited partnerships Types of partnerships General partnership Limited partnership Limited liability partnership Source of partnership law The main body of law governing partnerships is the Partnership Act, No 16 of 2012 The Act governs general and limited partnerships only. Limited liability partnerships are governed by the Limited Liability Partnership Act, 2011. There will often be a written agreement between the partners setting out the rights and duties of the partners between themselves. The Act sets out those provisions which will apply if no specific agreement is entered into. This is known as the ‚Äúdefault rule‚Äù (s2). Who is a partner? The partners in a partnership
... [Content truncated]

WWW.BRS.GO.KE PRE-INSOLVENCY MORATORIUM BY: DIANA MUMO, SENIOR ASSISTANT OFFICIAL RECEIVER A new provision under the Insolvency Act, 2015 has been introduced giving reprieve to distressed businesses. The purpose of the provision referred to as Pre-Insolvency moratorium is meant to prevent creditors from taking an enforcement action against a company while it considers its options for rescue, whether by new investment, formulating a restructuring plan, refinancing, sale or other rescue option available. What these provisions do is that they give time to the company to analyse its options. The process leaves the affairs of the company in the control of the company/directors under the supervision of a licenced Insolvency Practitioner who acts as a Monitor. The moratorium takes effect upon filing of Documents in Court and lasts for 30 days from and including the day on which the moratorium takes effect and may extended by Court for a further 30 days or more. The court shall endorse the copies of the documents filed and shall issue an Order recognising the moratorium in the Form provided under the Insolvency regulations. To obtain a pre-insolvency moratorium, the Directors have to file with the Court, among other documents, a statement from the Monitor indicating that the proposed moratorium has a reasonable prospect of achieving its aims and the Company has sufficient funds available to it during the proposed moratorium to enable it to carry on its business. What pre-insolvency m
... [Content truncated]

Primer on Guarantees in Kenya and English Law 1. Introduction to Guarantees A guarantee is a legal promise made by one party (the guarantor) to fulÔ¨Åll the obligations or settle the debt of another party (the principal debtor) if the latter fails to do so. Guarantees are commonly used in commercial transactions to provide security and assurance to creditors. They play a crucial role in risk mitigation and enhancing the creditworthiness of the debtor in the eyes of the creditor. In Kenya and under English law, guarantees are subject to various statutory provisions and case law principles that shape their formation, execution, and enforcement. 2. Legal Framework Governing Guarantees In Kenya, the legal framework governing guarantees is largely based on English common law, with certain statutory provisions that align with local practices. The key legal instruments include: The Law of Contract Act (Cap. 23, Kenya) This Act requires guarantees to be in writing and signed by the guarantor. Section 3(1) mandates that for a guarantee to be enforceable, there must be written evidence signed by the party to be charged with it. Section 3(1) of the LCA states: ‚ÄúNo suit shall be brought whereby to charge the defendant upon any special promise to answer for the debt, default or miscarriages of another person unless the agreement upon which such suit is brought, or some memorandum or note thereof, is in writing and signed by the party to be charged therewith or some other person thereunto by
... [Content truncated]

Chapter One Sources of Law in Kenya 1.1 What is Law? The world of commerce and industry is founded on distinct pillars of social order. The establishment and regulation of the various institutions of society depend upon frameworks of rules to which they owe their existence and from which they draw their power and legitimacy. These rules are promulgated and enforced in accordance with a defined legal system. As has been rightly observed, ‚Äú[i]n order to ensure good order and the smooth running of society, it is necessary to formulate general rules which work well enough in the majority of cases.‚Äù1 Accordingly, there is need in every society to promulgate normative rules, principles and doctrines to regulate social order. Such rules and principles must be effectively binding in the sense that derogation from them, or breach thereof, attracts penal or other remedial consequences. The assemblage of these doctrines, principles, rules, and regulations, may, in very loose terms, be collectively referred to as law. Glanville Williams defines law as ‚Äú‚Ä¶ the body of principles recognized and applied by the state in the administration of justice ‚Ä¶‚Äù According to him, it consists of ‚Äú‚Ä¶ the rules recognized and acted on by courts of justice.‚Äù2 In this sense, law includes all rules of conduct enacted by Parliament or established by court decisions in conformity with fundamental principles and doctrines recognized as essential for the administration of justice. Full Path: /home/ngobiro/projects/pngobiro.github.io/colleges/ksl/Advocates Training Program/ATP 108 - Commercial Transactions/Notes/99_Miscellaneous/Regulation of the National Payment System in Kenya.pdf

--- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
Regulation of the National Payment System in Kenya The National Payments Systems in Kenya are governed by a combination of laws, regulations, and guidelines issued by various regulatory bodies. The key legislative and regulatory frameworks include: 1. National Payment System Act, 2011 ‚Ä¢ This Act is the primary legislation governing the operation of payment systems in Kenya. It provides for the regulation and supervision of payment systems and payment service providers by the Central Bank of Kenya (CBK). 2. National Payment System Regulations, 2014 ‚Ä¢ These regulations, made under the National Payment System Act, provide detailed requirements for licensing, oversight, and operations of payment systems and service providers. They address issues such as the licensing process, operational standards, and compliance requirements. 3. Central Bank of Kenya Act, Cap 491 ‚Ä¢ The CBK Act empowers the Central Bank of Kenya to formulate and implement policies that ensure the stability of the national payment system. It gives CBK the authority to regulate, supervise, and oversee payment systems in Kenya. 4. Kenya Information and Communications Act, 1998 ‚Ä¢ This Act regulates electronic transactions, including mobile money and other digital payment systems. It establishes the legal framework for electronic commerce and transactions. 5. Proceeds of Crime and Anti-Money Laundering Act, 2009 (POCAMLA) ‚Ä¢ POCAMLA provides for measures to combat money laundering and the Ô¨Ånancing of terrorism. Payment
... [Content truncated]

Full Path: /home/ngobiro/projects/pngobiro.github.io/colleges/ksl/Advocates Training Program/ATP 108 - Commercial Transactions/Notes/99_Miscellaneous/SHAREHOLDERS-AGREEMENTS.docx

SHAREHOLDERS‚Äô AGREEMENT The Statutory Contract The company‚Äôs constitution comprised in its Articles of Association (the Articles) forms the basis of a statutory contract between the shareholders and the company and between the shareholders themselves. It is binding upon anyone who acquires shares and can be enforced by a shareholder if there is a breach of his rights as a shareholder. It cannot be set aside for misrepresentation or undue influence and the court cannot imply any terms into it. Moreover, it can be amended by a 75% majority of shareholders present and voting at a members meeting rendering vulnerable a minority shareholder. The Articles are a public document and accordingly, available for anyone to obtain a copy from the Companies Registry. A shareholders‚Äô agreement on the other hand is a private document. What is it? A shareholders‚Äô agreement is a contract between the shareholders in a private limited company. It is intended to regulate the relationship between the shareholders. It may specify things which shareholders must do and may specify things which they must not do. The company may also be a party to the agreement which is particularly important if the intention is to bind the company to the provisions of any such agreement. The provisions of the agreement will be legally binding and enforceable by all parties to it. Purpose The primary purpose of a shareholders‚Äô agreement is to record the intention of the parties as regards the business. This is likely t
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

SHARES, AND CONVERSION OF COMPANIES SHARESBoth public and private companies have sharesRepresent the ownership or interest in the company (Share certificate proof of title)Only apply in respect of companies limited by sharesThey represent a value preposition except for nominal share capital Classes of sharesOrdinary SharesPreferential SharesTreasury SharesRedeemable SharesEmployee SharesBonus SharesThe shares or other interest of a member in a company are personal property and are not in the nature of real estate (s.323) Allotment of Shares (s.327,328) Shares in a limited company having a share capital are each required to have a fixed nominal value. Shares in a limited company having a share capital are required to be denominated in shillings. a company that has a share capital shall ensure that each of its shares is distinguished by an appropriate distinguishing number. If a private company has only one class of shares, the directors may exercise any power of the company‚Äî (a) to allot shares of that class; or (b) to grant rights to subscribe for or to convert any security into such shares, except to the extent that they are prohibited from doing so by the company's articles. Regulation 7 and 8 and the Second Schedule of the Capital Markets (Takeovers and Mergers) Regulations, 2002 NOTE: The Offeror shall within fourteen (14) days from the date of serving the Offeror‚Äôs Statement pursuant to Regulation 4(4) submit to the Authority for approval, the Take-over Offer Document in relation to the take-over offer which shall include the information contained in the Second Schedule of the Regulations or such other information as the Authority may require. The take-over offer document shall be served by the offeror on the offeree within five (5) days from the date of approval of the take-over offer document by the Authority. The Offeror shall disclose in the Takeover Offer document all such information as the offeree shareholders and their professional advisers would reasonably require. THE BANK CUSTOMER RELATIONSHIP AND NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS A LECTURE PRESENTED AT THE KENYA SCHOOL OF LAW DANSON KIRITU, ADVOCATE LEGAL MANAGER, HEAD OFFICE & INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS KCB BANK KENYA LIMITED What is a 'Bank' A bank is a financial institution licensed to receive deposits and issue financial facilities including loans, guarantees and letters of credit. Banks may also provide financial services, such as wealth management, currency exchange and safe deposit boxes. There are two types of banks: commercial/retail banks and investment banks. In this country, banks are regulated by the central bank. Commercial banks are typically concerned with managing withdrawals and receiving deposits as well as supplying short-term loans to individuals and small businesses. Consumers primarily use these banks for basic checking and savings accounts, certificates of deposit (CDs) and home mortgages. Examples of commercial banks include KCB Bank, Barclays Bank of Kenya and Cooperative Bank Investment banks focus on providing corporate clients with services such as underwriting and assisting with merger and acquisition (M&A) activity. KCB Capital, Dyer and Blair, Kingdom Securities are examples of investment banks. Central banks are chiefly responsible for currency stability, controlling inflation and monetary policy and overseeing money supply. TAKEOVERS Definitions Takeover is defined by the company‚Äôs Act as an offer to acquire shares in a company. According to the black‚Äôs law dictionary, a take overtakes place when a company buys another to improve profits, client access and assets. It can be friendly or hostile Also defined under the Takeover and merges regulations 2002 as general offer to acquire all voting shares in the offeree/target Company. Target/ Offeree Company. This is a company whose shares are subject to takeover offer. In relation to a takeover scheme or a takeover offer means a listed company on a securities exchange with shares to which the scheme or offer relates. Takeover bids consists of an offer from an acquirer i.e. Company A to the shareholders of Company B (Target company) to acquire their shares for a consideration. The consideration may be in monetary form or securities of the offeror or both. Acquirer/ offeror- Person or company making a takeover offer. Offer period- The period from and including the date of the offer and ending with the time the offer can no longer be accepted. Mandatory bid- Permits non-controlling shareholders to exit the company at a fair price upon change of control. Holder of shares- Debentureholders or person who holds securities of a company that are convertible into shares of the company. Tax Revision Chart Government Uses of Taxes in Kenya a. Redistribution of income Kenya has a progressive tax structure whereas income increases one pays a higher percentage od taxes, since high income earners pay slightly more than the low earners taxation enables the redistribution of incomes in the economy. b. Controlling externalities An externality refers to a bi-product or consequence of activity (commercial or industrial) felt by a third party, like pollution. In a bid to discourage negative externalities such as noise pollution, the government can impose a tax. c. Revenue generation By taxing citizens, the government can generate money that can be spent to improve the lives of the citizens or to cater for any other government expenditure. Some areas where the government uses taxes include the provision of social welfare and infrastructure development. d. Finance debts A fraction of the revenues collected go to financing the debts accrued by the government e. FILE #391: The Business Judgment Rule as an Immunity Doctrine.pdf
====================================================================================================
Full Path: /home/ngobiro/projects/pngobiro.github.io/colleges/ksl/Advocates Training Program/ATP 108 - Commercial Transactions/Notes/99_Miscellaneous/The Business Judgment Rule as an Immunity Doctrine.pdf
File Size: 330,578 bytes

--- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
William & Mary Business Law Review Volume 4 | Issue 2 Article 5 The Business Judgment Rule as an Immunity Doctrine Lori McMillan Repository Citation Lori McMillan, The Business Judgment Rule as an Immunity Doctrine, 4 Wm. & Mary Bus. L. Rev. 521 (2013), http://scholarship.law.wm.edu/wmblr/vol4/iss2/5 Copyright c 2013 by the authors. This article is brought to you by the William & Mary Law School Scholarship Repository. http://scholarship.law.wm.edu/wmblr THE BUSINESS JUDGMENT RULE AS AN IMMUNITY DOCTRINE LORI MCMILLAN* ABSTRACT The business judgment rule is a judicially created doctrine that protects directors from personal civil liability for the decisions they make on behalf of a corporation. In today‚Äôs era of corporate scandals, global financial meltdowns, and directorial malfeasance, it has become especially important in setting the bar for when directors are appropriately responsible to shareholders for their actions. Traditionally the business judgment rule has been regarded as a standard of liability, although it has never really been explored or enunciated as such. This view determines eligibility for business judgment rule protection of a directorial decision after an examination of certain preconditions. An alternate view has developed that posits the business judgment rule is actually an abstention doctrine, and should be applied automatically absent the establishment of the same preconditions as the liability standard approach, only to be used as nullifying factor
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

The Company as a Criminal: Comparative Examination of some Trends and Challenges Relating to Criminal Liability of Corporate Persons George O. Otieno Ochich1 2008. 1. ABSTRACT As sophistication engulfs the business sphere, an increasing number of individuals prefer to incorporate their business enterprises into companies to facilitate their enjoyment of the advantages of corporate trading.1 That trend has seen many activities that were previously the preserve of natural persons being increasingly undertaken under the veil of incorporation. Incidental to such activities are bound to be those that have criminal dimensions. Of course, there exist a whole lot of persons who would seek to use the veil of incorporation to disguise their individual criminal intents and acts. Thus, in the pursuit of their business objectives companies engage in criminal activities, some of which are deliberate while others are merely incidental to their mandate. While certain such criminal activities may be blamed on the individuals who comprise the company, others are attributable to none other than the company itself. As the number of corporations increases and their involvement in diverse spheres of life expands, the need to call them to account for their criminal wrongdoing equally grows. This development poses new and complex challenges to criminal law as traditionally understood. Various jurisdictions have had to expand or reorient their perspectives of criminal responsibility in response to th
... [Content truncated]

The Key Elements of Insurance Contracts Offer and Acceptance An insurance contract begins with an application. You work with an agent or broker and provide the necessary information via their application form. If your offer meets the insurance company‚Äôs criteria, the company may accept it. You might have to pay your first premium before they will accept it, as is the case when you apply for property or liability insurance. Most of the time, the insurance agent can accept the offer for the company they represent. They issue a temporary contract, or binder, which activates your coverage immediately. After the company reviews your application, they issue a formal, detailed policy. If an agent cannot bind the insurance company, your application goes to the insurance company and you do not have coverage until acceptance. This might require making your first payment of your premium, such as when you apply for life insurance. Some companies also require a medical examination and will only activate coverage on the date you pass the exam. Legal Consideration Consideration is the agreed value between you and your insurance company. For you, it means you agree to abide by the contract and pay premiums. For the insurance company, it means they‚Äôll pay if you submit a claim for a covered loss. Competent Parties Only legally competent parties can enter into an insurance contract. This means you must be of legal age of majority, of sound mind, and not disqualified from the contract by law. T
... [Content truncated]

Pierce insisted that he didn‚Äôt have the sheep anymore. They now belonged to Twyne, to whom Pierce also owed money. Yes, Pierce‚Äôs mark was on the sheep and he had recently sheared some of them and even sold others. From the records we can infer that Pierce and Twyne were neighbors and perhaps friends, and that Pierce didn‚Äôt mind repaying Twyne, but he objected to paying anything (sheep or money) to C. Pierce had apparently heard in advance of the sheriff ‚Äôs visit and had given everything he owned ‚Äì his sheep, clothes and other personal possessions ‚Äì by deed to Twyne.2 As a result, there was nothing for the sheriff to recover. All of Pierce‚Äôs property was beyond the sheriff ‚Äôs reach, thus depriving C of any repayment. The cross-border insolvency regime in Kenya. To what extent has the Kenyan regime followed the UNCITRAL implementation guidelines? BY: NDWIGA SUSAN NJERI INTRODUCTION National insolvency laws provide treatment and remedy to the issues arising out of the corporate restructuring drives within the jurisdictions of the national laws. The law relating to insolvency that is operative in Kenya has remained in force without any substantive reform for about five decades. The changing economic climate coupled with the drive by multilateral institutions for institutionalization of the rule of law and good governance in developing countries has created a pressing need for amelioration of insolvency law and cross-border insolvency law in Kenya. The initiative is undertaken within the broad context of facilitating growth of trade and investment and foreign direct investment in particular with a view to reducing poverty. Cross-border insolvency in Kenya is a new creature that came along after the enactment of the new Insolvency Act. Cross-border insolvency deals with regulation of how debtors who owe debts and have assets in another country or creditors should be treated. It is more concerned with insolvency of companies which operate in more than one country rather than individual bankruptcy. The United Nations Commission on International Trade Law (UNCITRAL) has come with the Model Law for the cross border mergers & acquisitions exercise. It was established in 1966 as a subsidiary body of
... [Content truncated]

Primer: Trust Deeds under Common Law Introduction Trust deeds are foundational documents in commercial law, used in a wide range of transactions to create and manage trusts. Trusts allow one party (the trustee) to hold and manage assets on behalf of others (the beneÔ¨Åciaries). Trust deeds formalize this arrangement by setting out the terms and conditions governing the trust. Understanding the structure and application of trust deeds is essential for commercial lawyers, particularly in complex Ô¨Ånancial and real estate transactions. 1. Trusts under English Law DeÔ¨Ånition of a Trust A trust is a legal relationship where a trustee holds assets for the beneÔ¨Åt of the beneÔ¨Åciaries. Trusts are a key component of English law, reÔ¨Çecting the division between legal and equitable ownership. The trustee has legal ownership, but they must act in the interest of the beneÔ¨Åciaries, who hold the equitable (or beneÔ¨Åcial) interest. Key Components of a Trust ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß ¬ß Settlor: The person who creates the trust by transferring property to the trustee. Trustee: The individual or entity holding and managing the trust property. BeneÔ¨Åciary: The person(s) beneÔ¨Åting from the trust. Trust Property: The assets or property held in the trust. Types of Trusts ¬ß ¬ß Express Trusts: Created intentionally by the settlor. Can be Ô¨Åxed (speciÔ¨Åc beneÔ¨Åciaries and interests) or discretionary (trustees decide how to distribute assets). Implied Trusts: Resulting from actions or circumstances, rather than explicit intentions. T
... [Content truncated]

SETTING UP OF A TRUST IN KENYA A trust is a legal arrangement through which property is held by a third-party for the benefit of another party, called the beneficiary. The person who creates the trust is the ‚Äúsettlor." The settlor must transfer her property to the trust, which is then handled and administered by the trustee, or administrator, although the settlor may reserve specific powers to herself with respect to the trust. A trust deed is the document that details the trust's terms and conditions and lists the parties involved. In a trust deed, the settlor is simply the creator of the trust. Registration of a charitable trust or a foundation in Kenya generally requires the following: 1. Name of the Trust or Foundation 2. Main Object of the Trust. Charitable trust must fall in at least one of the following categories: ÔÇ∑ Relief of poverty e.g. homes & orphanages, hospitals ÔÇ∑ Advancement of education ÔÇ∑ Advancement of religion ÔÇ∑ Advancement of Health or saving of lives ÔÇ∑ Advancement of environmental protection or improvement ÔÇ∑ Trusts beneficial to the community not falling under the preceding heads e.g. advancing art, culture, racial harmony. 3. Name of the Settlor/ Donor ÔÇ∑ Full name, copy of ID card/passport ; If company, a copy of the certificate of registration ÔÇ∑ Address ÔÇ∑ Domicile and residence 4. Beneficiaries: This could be a class of persons in the society e.g. bright needy children; orphans between 0-10 years or an orphanage, cancer patients, women etc. There is also
... [Content truncated]

====================================================================================================
Full Path: /home/ngobiro/projects/pngobiro.github.io/colleges/ksl/Advocates Training Program/ATP 108 - Commercial Transactions/Notes/99_Miscellaneous/WaKimani Commercial Notes.pdf

--- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
COMPANIES ‚óè The Companies Act, 2015 Section 3 defines a company as "a company formed and registered under this Act or an existing company." ‚óè This definition is vague. In legal theory, a company can be defined as an association of a member of persons who come together for some common object or objects. (In ordinary usage it is associated with economic purposes or gain). ‚óè A company is an artificial legal person created by complying with the provisions of the Companies Act, 2015 of the Laws of Kenya. Fundamental concepts of company law 1. The concept of legal personality ‚óè Legal personality means that the law recognizes certain persons as having certain legal rights and duties which can be enforced by the courts. ‚óè Apart from human beings, the law also accords legal personality to corporations. A corporation is an artificial person created by law, and once it comes into being it is treated by law as a person in its own right, and is independent from the individual members who compose it. ‚óè Corporate personality thus encompasses the capacity of a corporation to have a name of its own, to sue and be sued, and the right to purchase, sell, lease, and mortgage its property in its own name. In addition, property cannot be taken away from a corporation without due process of the law. 2. The concept of limited liability ‚óè Liability means the extent to which a person can be made to account by law. One can be made accountable either for the full amount of their debts or else pay towards
... [Content truncated]
--- END EXCERPT ---

TAKEOVERS Definitions Takeover is defined by the company‚Äôs Act as an offer to acquire shares in a company. According to the black‚Äôs law dictionary, a take overtakes place when a company buys another to improve profits, client access and assets. It can be friendly or hostile Also defined under the Takeover and merges regulations 2002 as general offer to acquire all voting shares in the offeree/target Company. Target/ Offeree Company. This is a company whose shares are subject to takeover offer. In relation to a takeover scheme or a takeover offer means a listed company on a securities exchange with shares to which the scheme or offer relates. Takeover bids consist of an offer from an acquirer i.e. Company A to the shareholders of Company B (Target company) to acquire their shares for a consideration. The consideration may be in monetary form or securities of the offeror or both. Acquirer/ offeror- Person or company making a takeover offer. Offer period- The period from and including the date of the offer and ending with the time the offer can no longer be accepted. Mandatory bid- Permits non-controlling shareholders to exit the company at a fair price upon change of control. Holder of shares- Debentureholders or person who holds securities of a company that are convertible into shares of the company. Shares- Shares (other than relevant treasury shares) that have been allotted on the date of the offer. Firm timetable- takeovers are quite disruptive to the company business there

Structure of Kenya‚Äôs Financial Sector ARIEMBA JARED MOGAKA SOUTH EASTERN UNIVERSITY COLLEGE (A CONSTITUENT COLLEGE OF THE UNIVERSITY OF NAIROBI) BACHELOR OF COMMERCE LECTURE II ÔÇó The financial market deals with the purchase and sale of money. It therefore involves obtaining money from those who have more than they need by attracting it either as: ÔÇ° deposits through commercial banks; ÔÇ° premiums through insurance companies; ÔÇ° units or shares through Mutual Funds ÔÇ° or shares or bonds through investment banks. ÔÇó The money drawn from the savers is then pooled and allocated throughout the economy by giving it out as loans, common or corporate bonds, treasury bills inter alia. ÔÇó The financial market in Kenya can be said to have two major functions: ÔÇ° it allocates money capital through identifying those with surplus funds, attracting the funds into a pool and then distributing them to those who need to spend more than they have. ÔÇ° The other function of the financial market is the distribution of the economic risk through the creation and distribution of securities. ÔÇó Principally, financial market in Kenya comprise: ÔÇ° commercial banks, ÔÇ° non-bank financial institutions, ÔÇ° mortgage companies, ÔÇ° forex bureaus, ÔÇ° development finance institutions, ÔÇ° pension schemes, ÔÇ° SACCOs ÔÇ° the insurance sector and the stock market. GUARANTEES A contract of guarantee, is defined as ‚ÄúA contract to perform the promise, or discharge the liability of a third person in case of his defaults‚Äù. Example: A advances a loan of ksH.10,000 to B, and C promises A that if B does not repay the loan, I will repay it. This is a contract of guarantee. It involves three parties namely, 1. Surety, who gives the guarantee. 2. Principal Debtor, in respect of whose default the guarantee is given. 3. Creditor, to whom the guarantee is given. Example: A supplies goods to B on C‚Äôs guaranteeing payment by B to A. This means that if B does not pay, C would be liable to pay. This is a ‚ÄúContract of Guarantee‚Äù. Here B is the principal debtor, C is the surety and A is the creditor. A guarantee may be either ‚Äúoral‚Äù or ‚Äúwritten‚Äú. Just like any other contract, it should also fulfill all the essentials of a valid contract. As stated already, three parties are involved in a contract of guarantee. At the same time, there are three collateral contracts also namely, 1. As between A and B [A supplies goods to B on credit who promises that he would pay]. 2. As between A and C [C gives guarantee the price of goods, I will pay]. 3. As between C and B [C indemnifies A in case of B‚Äôs default in paying the amount to A). Essentials of a Contract of Guarantee 1. Concurrence of All the Parties All the three parties namely, the principal debtor, the creditor and the surety must agree to make such a contract. 2. Liability In a contract of guarantee, liabil

International Commercial Law and Arbitration Conference International Commercial Dispute Resolution and the Place of Judicial Power Chief Justice Robert French AC 22 August 2013, Sydney It is an old saw that the more things change the more they remain the same. While it no doubt illuminates a truth about constants in life, it also has the ring of nonsense about it. No more so than in the areas of interest to this Conference. In the delivery of legal services and in dispute resolution things are changing at a fast pace and nowhere at a faster pace than in the field of global trade and commerce which increasingly interpenetrates domestic trade and commerce. Those changes and changes in resolution of commercial disputes challenge long-established perspectives. One of those perspectives concerns the place of the judicial powers of the Commonwealth and the States in the field of international commercial disputes, which are constitutionally within the jurisdiction of Australian courts to resolve. FILE #438: reverse merger.docx
====================================================================================================
Full Path: /home/ngobiro/projects/pngobiro.github.io/colleges/ksl/Advocates Training Program/ATP 108 - Commercial Transactions/Notes/99_Miscellaneous/reverse merger.docx

--- CONTENT EXCERPT ---
A reverse merger or reverse takeover is a transaction in which a private company acquires or merges with (or into) a public ‚Äúshell‚Äù company to become publicly listed. The primary motivation for such a transaction is that it is much less expensive than a full IPO with its extensive Registration Statement (and disclosures) and the detailed guidance and approvals with the CAPITAL Markets Authority or similar agencies in other jurisdictions. Another advantage is likely the ability to not hire an underwriter for the offeror‚Äôs stock. As a result, the reverse merger is a way to enter the Kenyan capital market without engaging an underwriter and investment banker. The public company target in this type of transaction is usually a ‚Äúshell‚Äù company since all that exists of such company is its organizational structure with little or no active operations, assets, or liabilities, but listed on a nationally recognized stock exchange and not necessarily actively traded. A principal assumption for undertaking this kind of transaction is that the private company does not need liquidity or capital access immediately. While the process of going public and raising capital is combined in an IPO, in a reverse merger/takeover, these two functions are separate. So, a reverse merger/takeover is not a capital-raising event although the transaction could involve the filing of a Registration Statement or other securities regulatory filings once certain steps are complete. Once the private company identif

SHARE AGREEMENT Definition A shareholders‚Äô agreement is essentially a corporate pre-nuptial agreement. A shareholders‚Äô agreement is, as you might expect, an agreement between the shareholders of a company. It can be between all or, in some cases, only some of the shareholders (like, for instance, the holders of a particular class of share). Its purpose is to protect the shareholders‚Äô investment in the company, to establish a fair relationship between the shareholders and govern how the company is run. The agreement will: ÔÇ∑ set out the shareholders‚Äô rights and obligations; ÔÇ∑ regulate the sale of shares in the company; ÔÇ∑ describe how the company is going to be run; ÔÇ∑ provide an element of protection for minority shareholders and the company; and ÔÇ∑ define how important decisions are to be made. Usually, it is best to put a shareholders‚Äô agreement in place when the company is formed and issue the first shares. In fact, it can be a positive exercise to ensure there is common understanding of shareholders‚Äô expectations of the business. At that point, the shareholders should, as far as is possible, be of a similar mind about what they expect to offer and get from the company. Indeed if the differences of opinion between the investors at this stage are too strong to form a shareholders‚Äô agreement, it is likely to ring warning bells about the nature of their future working relationship. The investors may choose to defer discussing a shareholders‚Äô agreement in order to get on with the 
... [Content truncated]
